<div><b>Osteopetrosis</b> is due to <i>poor</i> <u>osteo-</u>{{c1::<u>clast</u>}} function, impairing the ability to <u>{{c2::remodel}}</u> bone</div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div>	"- Can be due to mutations in Carbonic Anhydrase II (required for production of H+ for the resorption pit)<div><br /></div><div>- bone remodeling strengthens and repairs bone through the removal of old bone by osteoclasts and replacement with new bone by osteoblasts<br /><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-39247411150849.jpg"" /></div></div>"					"<img src=""tmp189Qqw_1566160514431.png""><br/>"	"<div><br><div><br></div><div><img src=""paste-656941017728860.jpg""></div></div><img src=""tmpyC6wzu_1566160514431-f9a0c0ffd11e61bce4e7b2907314c94a32b15fb7.png"">"				"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::03_Pathology::01_Bone_Disorders #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::10_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v7::#Pathoma::18_MSK::01_Skeletal_System #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::03_Osteoblasts_&_Osceoclasts #AK_Step1_v7::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Pathology #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::03_Pathology::01_Bone_Disorders #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::10_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v9::#Pathoma::18_MSK::01_Skeletal_System #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::03_Osteoblasts_&_Osceoclasts #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::11_Pathology::04_Musculoskeletal_Pathology::01_Bone_Disorders #AK_Step1_v9::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Pathology"

A {{c1::Baker cyst}} is a collection of {{c2::synovial fluid}} in the {{c3::popliteal fossa}} in the {{c4::gastrocnemius-semimembranous}} bursa.	"<div>Causes a proximal tibial nerve lesion (sensory + motor deficits), rupture mimics a DVT</div><div><img src=""paste-465071943712769.jpg""></div><div>popliteal fossa is defined as the diamond shaped fossa outlined by the four muscles<br></div><div><img src=""paste-94910187307009.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::01_Knee #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::01_Knee #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Baker cyst forms in the setting of {{c1::chronic degenerative or inflammatory joint}} disease.	"Like OsteoArthritis<br><div><img src=""paste-465067648745473.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::01_Knee #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

The rotator cuff muscles are innervated primarily by {{c1::C5-C6::(nerve roots)}}	"<div><img src=""paste-72516227825665.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The rotator cuff muscles are:<div>1. {{c1::supraspinatus}}</div><div>2. {{c2::infraspinatus}}</div><div>3. {{c3::teres minor}}</div><div>4. {{c4::subscapularis}}</div>	"<div><br /></div><img src=""paste-62341450301441.jpg"" /><br /><div><br /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::teres minor}} (rotator cuff muscle) is innervated by the {{c2::axillary}} nerve.	"<img src=""paste-11678016077825.jpg"" /><br /><div><img src=""paste-11665131175937.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::subscapularis}} (rotator cuff muscle) is innervated by the {{c2::upper and lower}} {{c3::subscapular (C5-C6)}} nerves (2).	"<div>The lower middle of the <u>posterior cord</u> gives off the upper and lower subscapular nerves (thus cutting the posterior cord mid way results in impairment of <u>internal rotation </u>/ adduction along with deltoid paralysis + wrist drop)</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-21805548961793.jpg"" /></div>"	"<img src=""paste-8d7245413658992a2380eee852fd8da3c097d39f.jpg"">"									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::supraspinatus}} {{c3::a<b>B</b>ducts}} the arm initially (from {{c2::0 to 15}}°).	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-465471375671297.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::02_Arm_abduction #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::1733 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::supraspinatus}} is the most commonly injured rotator cuff muscle, due to {{c2::trauma}} or {{c3::degeneration and impingement}} which leads to {{c4::tendinopathy or tear}}	"<div><i>Impingement of the tendon can occur between the humeral head and acromion process</i></div><div><br /></div><div>- This results in pain and tenderness at deltoid with weakness in active shoulder abduction</div><div>- in a tear, pain elicited with forcible forward elevation of arm (<u>Neer's Sign</u>)</div><div>- seen in <u>swimmers</u> and <u>throwers</u></div><div><u><br /></u></div><div><u><br /></u></div><div><u><img src=""paste-466003951616001.jpg"" /><img src=""paste-465471375671297.jpg"" /></u></div><div><u><img src=""paste-399784145846273.jpg"" /></u></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

Infraspinatus can also help extend shoulder									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::01_Rotator_cuff_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_16 #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Free120_2020_old_and_new #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::subscapularis}} (rotator cuff muscle) {{c2::a<b>D</b>ducts and medially (<i>internally</i>) rotates}} the arm (action). The {{c1::trapezius}} abducts the arm {{c2::&gt;90}}° and is innervated by the {{c3::Spinal Accessory (CNXI)}} nerve	"<div>The sternocleidomastoid is also innervated by CN XI</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-474628245946369.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::02_Arm_abduction #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::11772 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

Carpal tunnel syndrome is due to entrapment of the {{c2::<b>median</b>}} nerve between the {{c1::transverse carpal}} ligament and the {{c1::carpal}} bones	"transverse carpal ligament is also known as the <u>flexor retinaculum</u>; <b>can surgically incise</b> the transverse carpal ligament to relieve symptoms<div><br /></div><div><br /><div><u><img src=""paste-245620589723649.jpg"" /></u></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::02_Wrist_&_Hand_Injuries #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
{{c1::Carpal tunnel}} syndrome leads to atrophy of the {{c2::<b>thenar</b> eminence}}, but spares {{c3::sensation}} because the {{c4::<b>palmar cutaneous branch</b>}} enters the hand external to the carpal tunnel	- thus superficial laceration of the palm (damage to the recurrent branch of the median nerve) will NOT have loss of sensation<div><br></div><div>Still finger numbness but palm is fine</div>	"<img src=""paste-0ae16c01214c5050eea000648e305aff311b2b72.jpg"">"									"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::02_Wrist_&_Hand_Injuries #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
A positive {{c1::Phalen}} maneuver is when {{c2::90 degree flexion of the wrist causes tingling}}. {{c1::<b>Guyon canal</b>}} <b>syndrome</b> is due to compression of the {{c2::ulnar}} nerve at the {{c3::wrist}} or {{c3::hand}}.											"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::02_Wrist_&_Hand_Injuries #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Guyon canal syndrome is classically seen in {{c1::cyclists}} due to {{c1::pressure from handlebars}}											"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::02_Wrist_&_Hand_Injuries #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the {{c1::axillary (C5-C6)::roots}} nerve presents as: <div>1. {{c2::flattened deltoid}} (appearance)</div><div>2. loss of {{c3::arm abduction at shoulder (&gt;15°)}} (action)</div><div>3. loss of {{c4::sensation over the deltoid muscle and lateral arm}} (sensation)</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-92058329022465.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-22329534971905.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_21 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::01_Upper_Trunk,_Axillary,_Musculocutaneous,_Suprascapular_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::08_Neuro::15_Dermatomes,_Myotomes_&_Clinical_Reflexes #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the {{c1::musculocutaneous}} nerve ({{c2::C5-C7}} (nerve roots)) is commonly due to {{c3::upper trunk compression}}.	"<div>Can also be due to a gun shot to the anterior biceps</div><div><br></div><img src=""paste-92054034055169.jpg""><img src=""paste-93814970646529.jpg"">"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::01_Upper_Trunk,_Axillary,_Musculocutaneous,_Suprascapular_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the {{c1::musculocutanous (C5-7)::roots}} nerve presents as:<div>1. loss of {{c2::forearm flexion}} and {{c2::forearm supination}} (action)</div><div>2. loss of {{c3::sensation over the lateral forearm}} (sensation)</div><div><br></div>	"- This can be caused by a <u>bullet shot</u> to the <b>anterior biceps</b><div><br /><div>*Musculocutaneous nerve <b>innervates</b> the </div><div> - coracobrachialis (flex and adduct arm at glenohumeral joint)</div><div> - biceps brachii (supinates the forearm, and flexes the forearm)</div><div> - brachialis (flexes the forearm)</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-92054034055169.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_18 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::01_Upper_Trunk,_Axillary,_Musculocutaneous,_Suprascapular_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"Injury to the {{c1::radial}} nerve ({{c2::C5-T1::(nerve roots)}}) can be caused by<div>- {{c3::midshaft}} fracture of the humerus</div><div>- compression of the {{c4::axilla}}</div><div>- ""{{c5::nursemaids}} elbow""<br /></div><div>- ""{{c6::finger}}"" drop</div>"	"<b>midshaft fracture of the humerus</b><div>- damage of the nerve in the spiral groove of the humerus; deep brachial artery is at risk of damage </div><div>- triceps brachii are spared and sensory nerves over posterior arm / forearm are unaffected (nerves that supply these have already been given off); thus <b>supination</b> is the most impacted</div><div><br></div><div><div><img src=""paste-12584254177281.jpg""></div></div><div><br></div><div><b>compression of the axilla</b></div><div>- due to incorrect use of crutches or passing out with arm over chair</div><div><br></div><div><b>Radial head subluxation</b> (<u>""nursemaids elbow""</u>)</div><div>- longitudinal traction applied to an extended an pronated arm (ex by nursemaid pulling vigorously at childs arm) can lead to <u>annular ligament</u><b> </b>slipping over the head of the radius and impinging deep radial nerve</div><div>- causes lateral elbow pain, weakness, no sensory deficits or wrist drop</div><div><br></div><div><b>Finger Drop</b></div><div>- repetitive pronation / supination of forearm (ex screwdriver use)</div><div>- palsy in deep radial nerve (extensors of finger and wrist)</div><div><b><br></b></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_18 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Compression of the axilla (resulting in radial nerve injury) can occur due to improper use of {{c1::crutches}} or {{c2::sleeping with an arm over a chair (aka Saturday night palsy)}}.	"<div><img src=""2016-11-17-1479412880-2672082-nerve9_1566160514431.PNG"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
{{c1::Radial}} nerve injury presents with:<div>1. {{c2::wrist drop, including loss of elbow, wrist, and finger extension}} (action)</div><div>2. {{c3::decreased grip strength (due to loss of wrist extension)}} (strength)</div><div>3. {{c4::loss of sensation over posterior arm/forearm and dorsal hand}} (sensation)</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-98255966830593.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_22 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
{{c2::Median}} nerve ({{c1::C5-T1::nerve roots}}) injury is commonly due to:<div><br></div><div>- {{c6::anteromedial}} {{c3::supracondylar}} fracture of the humerus ({{c3::proximal::proximal or distal?}} lesion)</div><div>- {{c4::carpal tunnel}} syndrome ({{c4::distal::proximal or distal?}} lesion)</div><div>- {{c5::wrist}} laceration ({{c5::distal::proximal or distal?}} lesion)</div>	"<div><br><img class="""" src=""paste-98251671863297.jpg"" style=""width: 606px;""></div><div><i><u><b>anteromedial supracondylar fracture of the humerus</b> </u></i></div><div><i>- supracondylar ridges are immediately proximal to lateral / medial epicondyles</i></div><div><i><img class="""" src=""paste-15951508537345.jpg"" style=""width: 458px;""></i></div><div><i>- most common pediatric elbow fracture </i></div><div><i>- typically caused by fall on outstretched elbow</i></div><div><i>- results in <u>sensory deficits in medial distribution, loss of thumb opposition, flexion of lateral fingers and lumbricals of 2nd / 3rd digits </u></i></div><div><i>- patients have a ""<b>pope's blessing""</b> hand sign upon patient making a fist</i></div><div><i>- b/c <u>brachial artery</u> is paired with median nerve here, brachial artery can be damaged resulting in <b>Volkmann's Ischemic Contracture</b> (uncontrolled flexion in hand muscles as flexors become fibrotic and short)</i></div><div><i>- MRI elucidates</i></div><div><i><br></i></div><div><i><img class="""" src=""paste-16114717294593.jpg"" style=""width: 372px;""></i></div><div><i><br></i></div><div><i><b><u>Carpal tunnel syndrome or wrist laceration </u></b></i></div><div><i><b>- </b>result in a ""median claw lesion"" (distal median nerve lesion - test distal lesion by having patients extend from a fist)</i></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"Special findings in a {{c1::<b>median</b>}} nerve injury include ""{{c1::Median}} Claw"" ({{c3::<i>distal lesion</i>}}) and ""{{c2::Pope's blessing}}"" ({{c3::<i>proximal lesion</i>}})"	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-52836687675393.jpg"" /></div><div>""median claw"" = ape hand</div>"	proximal is above elbow and distal is below									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Loss of muscle action in a {{c5::median}} nerve injury includes loss of:<div>1. {{c1::wrist flexion}}</div><div>2. {{c2::flexion of lateral fingers (2nd and 3rd digits)}}</div><div>3. {{c3::thumb opposition (not thumb flexion)}}</div><div>4. {{c4::lateral 2 lumbricals (2nd and 3rd digits)}}</div>	"<div><u>- NOTE - </u></div><div>-<b> thumb <u>adduction</u> is mediated by the deep ulnar nerve</b>, not the median nerve</div><div>- <i>reccurent branch of median nerve does thumb flexion</i></div><div><i><br></i></div><div><i><img src=""paste-121079423041537.jpg"" draggable=""false""></i></div>"	Flexor carpi radialis is innervated by the median nerve									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v7 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v7 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
In a {{c1::median}} nerve injury, sensation is lost over the:<br />1. {{c2::thenar eminence}}<div>2. {{c3::dorsal aspects of lateral 3 1/2 fingers (with proximal lesion)}}</div><div>3. {{c4::palmar aspects of lateral 3 1/2 fingers (with proximal lesion)}}</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-121083718008833_1454789051274.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_22 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"Injury to the {{c1::ulnar}} nerve ({{c2::C8-T1}} (nerve roots)) commonly occurs due to:<div><br /></div><div>- {{c3::fracture of the medial epicondyle of humerus}} {{c5::""funny bone""}} ({{c6::proximal}} lesion) </div><div><br /></div><div>- {{c4::fractured hook of hamate}} ({{c7::distal}} lesion) (from fall on outstretched hand - ""FOOSH"")</div>"	"-<i> Fracture of the medial epicondyle of the humerus results in ""OK gesture"" lesion (proximal ulnar lesion - test proximal lesion by having them make a fist)</i><div><i><br /></i></div><div><div><i>- Fracture of the hook of hamate results in ""Ulnar Claw"" lesion (distal ulnar lesion - test distal lesion by having them extend fingers from a fist)</i></div></div><div><i><br /></i></div><div><i><img src=""paste-23940147707905.jpg"" /></i></div>"	funny bone area is cubital tunnel syndrome									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"A special finding in a(n) {{c1::distal ulnar}} nerve injury: ""{{c2::ulnar claw}}"" on {{c3::digit <b>extension</b>}}"	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-113945482362881.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The wrist will deviate {{c1::radially}} upon {{c2::flexion}} in a {{c3::proximal}} {{c4::ulnar}} nerve lesion.	<div><i>Ulnar and Median nerve are responsible for wrist flexion, therefore if you lose one side it is more apparent on the other side</i></div>	Both the Ulnar and Median nerves flex the wrist via flexor carpi ulnaris and flexor carpi radialis respectively									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Loss of muscle action in an {{c1::ulnar}} nerve injury includes loss of:<div>1. {{c2::wrist flexion}}</div><div>2. {{c3::flexion of medial fingers (4th and 5th digits)}}</div><div>3. {{c4::abduction AND adduction of fingers (interossei)}}</div><div>4. {{c5::medial 2 lumbricals (4th and 5th digits)}}</div>	"<div>This is because the ulnar nerve innervates the flexor carpi ulnaris and the medial portion of the flexor digitorum profundus; once it enters the wrist (b/w hook of hamate and pisiform bone in Guyons canal) it divides into superficial ulnar and deep ulnar (deep intrinsic hand muscles)</div><img src=""paste-23935852740609.jpg""><br><div><img src=""paste-121079423041537.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
In a(n) {{c1::ulnar}} nerve injury, sensation is lost over the {{c2::medial 1 1/2 fingers}}, including the {{c3::hypothenar eminence}}.	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-121079423041537.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the {{c1::<b>recurrent branch of the median</b>}} <b>nerve</b> ({{c2::<b>C5-T1::</b>nerve roots}}) is commonly due to {{c3::<b>superficial laceration of the palm</b>}}.	"Motor deficits WITHOUT sensory loss (sensory branch comes off before)<br /><div><img src=""paste-121079423041537.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::04_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

{{c1::Thoracic outlet syndrome}} (condition) is commonly caused by:<div>- presence of a {{c2::cervical}} rib </div><div>- {{c3::Pancoast}} tumor</div><div>- {{c4::scalene}} muscular abnormalities / hypertrophy / trauma (ex repetitive overhead arm movements)</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-205892142235649.jpg"" /></div>"	A&nbsp;<b>Pancoast tumor</b>&nbsp;is a&nbsp;<b>tumor</b>&nbsp;of the pulmonary apex. <b>Thoracic outlet syndrome</b> presents with:<div><br /></div><div>- {{c2::<u>atrophy of intrinsic hand muscles</u>}} (due to loss of lower trunk nourishment)</div><div>- {{c3::<u>ischemia and pain</u>}} (due to artery compression)</div><div>- {{c4::<u>edema</u>}} (due to venous compression)</div><div>- and <u>Klumpke Palsy</u> (due to lower trunk compression)</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""medill_lg_thoutletsyndrome_1566160514431.png"" /><img src=""paste-207511344906241.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::04_Brachial_plexus_lesions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::04_Brachial_plexus_lesions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::04_Shoulder #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
{{c1::Winged scapula}} (condition) is due to a lesion of the long thoracic nerve that supplies the {{c2::serratus anterior}}	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-208911504244737.jpg"" /></div><div>serratus anterior originates on the surface of the first 8 ribs and inserts on medial border of the scapula</div><div>- functions to stabilize and rotate scapula upward</div><div>- acts with trapezius to rotate glenoid cavity superiorly, allowing complete abduction of arm over head from horizontal position</div><div>- anchors scapula to thoracic cage</div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::04_Brachial_plexus_lesions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Winged scapula is commonly caused by:<div>- {{c1::axillary node}} dissection after {{c1::mastectomy}} </div><div>- {{c2::stab wounds}}</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-208907209277441.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::04_Brachial_plexus_lesions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Winged scapula is due to loss of action of {{c1::the serratus anterior}} (muscle).	"<div><br /></div><i>Serratus anterior pulls the scapula against the rib cage. If you lose it, the scapula ""wings"" out.</i><br /><div><img src=""paste-208907209277441.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::04_Brachial_plexus_lesions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"<div>A long thoracic nerve lesion presents with: </div><div>#1 - inability to abduct arm above {{c1::horizontal position}}</div><div>#2 - inability to anchor {{c2::scapula}} to thoracic cage</div>"	"#1 - due to impaired rotation of scapular<div>#2 - due to paralysis of <b>serratus anterior</b>; presents with a <u>winged scapula</u></div><div><br /><div><img src=""paste-208907209277441.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::03_Brachial_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::04_Brachial_plexus_lesions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Clawing of the hand is best seen with {{c1::<u>distal</u>::(proximal or distal)}} lesions of the {{c2::median}} or {{c2::ulnar}} nerves.											"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::07_Distortions_of_the_hand #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::07_Distortions_of_the_hand #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Clawing of the hand is characterized by {{c1::<u>extension</u>::(flexion/extension)}} at the {{c2::MCP}} joint, {{c1::<u>flexion</u>::(flexion/extension)}} at the {{c2::PIP}} joint, and {{c1::<u>flexion</u>::(flexion/extension)}} at the {{c2::DIP}} joint.	<br /><div>Loss of either the median or ulnar nerve takes out the lumbricals (flexion at MCP joint) → unopposed flexion of PIP and DIP joints</div>										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::05_Hand #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::07_Distortions_of_the_hand #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::02_Lower_Trunk_&_the_Median_&_Ulnar_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"{{c1::""Ulnar claw"" hand}} (<i>distortion of the hand</i>) is most apparent when {{c2::<u>extending</u> fingers or at rest}}, and is due to a {{c3::<u>distal ulnar</u>}} nerve lesion." The {{c1::iliohypogastric}} nerve ({{c2::T12-L1::nerve roots}}) provides:<div><br><div>- <u>sensory</u> innervation to the {{c3::<b>suprapubic</b> <b>region</b>}} </div><div>- <u>motor</u> innervation to the {{c4::<b>transversus</b> <b>abdominis</b>}} and {{c4::<b>internal</b> <b>oblique</b>}}</div></div>	"<div>- the anterior branch innervates the skin over the puic region, lateral branch innervates the gluteal region</div><img src=""paste-15192cf840465650bbe69bc484e8172b70688281.jpg""><br><div><img src=""paste-32173600014337.jpg""></div><div><img src=""paste-46110768889857.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The iliohypogastric nerve is commonly injured due to <b>post abdominal surgery {{c1::sutures}}</b>	"Common injured in laparotomy, laparoscopic surgery, hernia surgery, hysterectomy, appendectomy<div>- in these surgeries, surgeons commonly make <b>transverse incisions</b> across lower abdomen or pelvis</div><div>- when the surgery is done and wound sutured, sutures may trap this nerve (especially at edge)</div><div><br /><div><img src=""paste-32177894981633.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the iliohypogastric nerve often presents as burning or tingling pain at the {{c1::surgical incision site}} radiating to the inguinal and suprapubic region	"If the anterior branch alone is injured (ex. appendectomy), there will be decreased sensation at the pubic region<br /><div><br /><div><img src=""paste-32173600014337.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::genitofemoral}} nerve ({{c2::L1-L2::(nerve roots)}}) provides:<div><br><div>- sensory innervation to the {{c3::scrotum/labia majora::genital area}} and {{c3::upper anterior thigh::femoral area}} </div><div>- motor innervation to the {{c4::cremaster}}</div></div>	"<div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_22 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c3::genitofemoral}} nerve is commonly injured due to <b>laparoscopic surgeries </b>for {{c1::<b>inguinal</b> <b>hernias</b>}} or by <b>{{c2::retractor blades}}</b> used in abdominal surgery	"-<b> Inguinal hernia surgery </b>is often done with laparoscopic surgery, the genitofemoral nerve traverses the inguinal hernia repair operative field<div><br /></div><div>- additionally, can be damaged by <b>retractor blades</b> in abdominal surgery<br /><div><img src=""paste-34303903793153.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_22 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the {{c1::genitofemoral}} nerve presents as decreased sensation of {{c2::upper anterior thigh}} and an absent {{c3::cremasteric reflex}}.	"Basically, the anterior thigh below the inguinal ligament<br><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div>"	cremasteric reflex is where stroking of the inner thigh causes scrotum to rise									"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_22 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::lateral femoral cutaneous}} nerve ({{c2::L2-L3}} (nerve roots)) provides sensory innervation to the {{c3::anterior and lateral thigh}} and motor innervation to the {{c4::<b>none</b>}}.	"<div><br /></div>NO motor innervation; don't be fooled!<br /><div><img src=""paste-34686155882497.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The lateral femoral cutaneous nerve is commonly injured (<b>meralgia paresthetica</b>) due to {{c1::tight clothing}}, {{c1::obesity}} or {{c1::pregnancy}}	"can be injured during hyperflexion of the thighs in the lithotomy position for pelvic surgery or a vaginal delivery<br /><div><img src=""paste-34681860915201.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

The <b>sciatic nerve i</b>s commonly injured due to:<div>- {{c1::a herniated disc}}</div><div>- {{c2::posterior hip dislocation}}</div><div>- {{c3::piriformis syndrome}}</div>	"<div>- can use the <b>straight leg test</b> to help screen (very sensitive)</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-41974715383809.jpg"" /></div>"	<div><div>Piriformis syndrome: A disorder in which the piriformis muscle in the buttocks irritates the sciatic nerve.</div></div><div><div><div></div><div>Symptoms include pain, tingling, or numbness in the buttocks and down the leg, which may worsen after sitting for a long time, climbing stairs, walking, or running.</div></div></div>									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::08_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::08_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::11727 #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::sciatic}} nerve splits into the {{c2::common peroneal/fibular}} nerve and the {{c2::tibial}} nerve	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-41974715383809.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::<u><b>common</b></u> peroneal}} nerve ({{c2::L4-S2}} (nerve roots)) provides:<div><br></div><div>- <u>sensory innervation</u> to the {{c3::dorsum of the foot}} </div><div>- <u>motor innervation</u> to the {{c4::short head biceps femoris::proximal}}, {{c4::tibialis anterior}}, and {{c5::extensor muscles of the foot}}.</div>	"<div>AKA common fibular nerve; this is the <b>most commonly injured</b> leg nerve</div><div><br /></div><div>**<b>common</b> does short head of the biceps femoris</div><div><br /></div><div>**<b>superficial</b> branch does lateral, <b>deep</b> branch does anterior</div><div><img src=""paste-43009802502145.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::common peroneal}} nerve is commonly injured due to<div><br></div><div>- {{c2::trauma}} </div><div>- {{c3::compression of the lateral aspect of the leg (ex Cast)}} </div><div>- a {{c4::fibular neck}} fracture</div>	"<div>compression at the lateral aspect of leg will compress the fibular neck</div><div><br /></div>AKA common fibular nerve<div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-695385269993473.jpg"" /><br /><div><img src=""paste-43254615638017.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Free120_2020_old_and_new #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the {{c1::common peroneal}} nerve presents as loss of sensation on {{c2::the dorsum of the foot}} and {{c3::foot drop}} (motor).	"<br /><div>AKA common fibular nerve</div><div><img src=""paste-43250320670721.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Free120_2020_old_and_new #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::1803 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Foot drop is characterized by a foot that is {{c1::inverted}} and {{c1::plantarflexed}} at rest.	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-43250320670721.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Foot drop is characterized by loss of {{c1::eversion}} and {{c1::dorsiflexion}} (movements) of the foot.	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-44122199031809.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
"Foot drop results in a ""{{c1::steppage}} gait"""	"<img src=""paste-230459355168769.jpg"" /><br /><div><img src=""paste-44117904064513.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::tibial}} nerve ({{c2::L4-S3}} (nerve roots)) provides:<div>- sensory innervation to the {{c3::sole of foot}} </div><div>- motor innervation to the {{c4::triceps surae (soleus)}}, {{c4::plantaris}}, {{c5::popliteus}}, {{c5::flexor muscles of the foot}}, and {{c6::tibialis posterior}}</div>	"- tibialis posterior is the major muscle involved in foot <b>inversion</b><br><div><img src=""paste-44362717200385.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-2bd033ab52b1153b72be2456586ccc8341b97f7b.jpg""><img src=""paste-1a953eed0cfe0de4a1e35e9c1cd4d876016dfeaa.jpg""><br></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The tibial nerve is commonly injured due to:<div>- {{c1::knee}} trauma</div><div>- {{c2::Baker cyst}} (proximal lesion) </div><div>- {{c3::tarsal tunnel syndrome}} (distal lesion)</div>	"<div>- Baker cyst is located in the popliteal fossa, can cause palsy either due to direct compression or indirectly after cyst rupture</div><div><br /></div><div>- Tarsal tunnel is made by flexor retinaculum and medial surfaces of talus and calcaneus; causes include exercise, flat feet, DM, neurofibromatosis, lipomas</div><div><br /></div><div><div><img src=""paste-44358422233089.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::01_Biochem::04_Genetics::05_Autosomal_dominant_diseases #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::01_Biochem::04_Genetics::05_Autosomal_dominant_diseases #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Distal tibial nerve lesions present with:<div><br /></div><div>- sensory loss over the {{c1::sole}} of the foot</div><div>- weakness of {{c2::intrinsic}} muscles of the foot</div>	"<div>- plantar flexion / inversion are intact as the nerves innervating these muscle groups have been given off</div><div><br /></div><div>- Distal tibial nerve lesions (ex. at the <b>tarsal tunnel</b>) include tarsal tunnel syndrome</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-44358422233089.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-46106473922561.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::superior gluteal}} nerve ({{c2::L4-S1::(nerve roots)}}) provides:<div><br /><div>- sensory innervation to the {{c3::<b>none</b>}} </div><div>- motor innervation to the {{c4::gluteus medius}}, {{c4::gluteus minimus}}, and {{c5::tensor fascia latae}}.</div></div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-22625887715329.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The superior gluteal nerve is commonly injured due to {{c1::intramuscular}} injection to the {{c1::upper medial}} gluteal region (iatrogenic).	"<div>this is because this area is right by where the superior gluteal nerve leaves the pelvis through the greater sciatic foramen above the level of the piriformis</div><div><br /></div>Thus inject into superolateral quadrant (anterolateral region) to avoid nerve injury<br /><div><img src=""paste-22621592748033.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Injury to the<b> superior gluteal nerve</b> presents with {{c7::trendelenburg sign}} and a gluteus medius {{c8::lurch}} 	"- Trendelenburg sign is caused by the weakness of the gluteus medius / minimus muscles causing the pelvis to sag toward the unaffected (contralateral) side when the patient stands on the affected leg<div><br /></div><div>- Gluteus medius lurch is seen when walking, as the patient will lean toward the affected (ipsilateral) side to compensate for hip drop</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-26285199851521.jpg"" /></div><div><br /><div><img src=""paste-23957327577089.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Trendelenburg sign is characterized by {{c2::tilting of the pelvis}} <i>{{c1::contralateral}}</i> to the lesion.	"<div>The lesion is on the ipsilateral side to which the patient is bearing weight</div><div><br /></div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-23953032609793.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Intramuscular injections in the buttocks should be given in the {{c1::anterolateral}} quadrant (specifically the {{c2::von Hochstetter}} triangle) to avoid injury to the superior gluteal nerve	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-25353191948289.jpg"" /></div><div><img src=""paste-115646289412097.jpg"" /><img src=""paste-23953032609793.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::inferior gluteal}} nerve ({{c2::L5-S2::(nerve roots)}}) provides:<div><br /></div><div>- sensory innervation to the <b>{{c3::none}}</b> </div><div>- motor innervation to the {{c4::gluteus maximus}}.</div>	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-25971667238913.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The inferior gluteal nerve is commonly injured due to {{c1::posterior hip dislocation}}.	"<br /><div><img src=""paste-25967372271617 (1).jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The {{c1::pudendal}} nerve ({{c2::S2-S4}} (nerve roots)) provides:<div>- sensory innervation to the {{c3::perineum}} </div><div>- motor innervation to the {{c4::external urethral}} and {{c4::anal sphincters}}</div>	"<div><i>S2-S4 keeps the penis off the floor!</i></div><div><img src=""paste-27526445400065.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::06_Repro::03_Pelvic_Floor #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
The pudendal nerve can be injured due to:<div>- stretching during {{c1::childbirth}}</div><div>- {{c2::saddle}} injury seen in cyclists</div>	"Cyclists apply long term pressure on saddle area, which can lead to pudendal nerve entrapment syndrome <div><br /></div><div>can also be caused by <b>saccrococcygeal teratoma</b></div><div><div><div><img src=""paste-27522150432769.jpg"" /></div></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::09_Lower_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::06_Lumbosacral_Plexus #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

In a <b>lumbar</b> disc herniation, the disc typically herniates into the {{c1::<u>vertebral</u> canal}}, affecting the {{c2::inferior::superior or inferior}} nerve	"<div><i>e.g. A disc herniation between L3 and L4 affects the L4 spinal nerve; NOT L3</i></div><div><i><br /></i></div><div><i><img src=""paste-5570572582913.jpg"" /></i></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::07_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::12_Signs_of_lumbosacral_radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::08_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
<b>Intervertebral discs</b> generally herniate {{c1::<u>posterolaterally</u>}} (direction) due to the <i>thin</i> {{c2::posterior longitudinal}} ligament and <i>thicker</i> {{c2::anterior longitudinal}} ligament											"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::07_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::12_Signs_of_lumbosacral_radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::08_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::07_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::12_Signs_of_lumbosacral_radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::08_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
An intervertebral disc herniation at the {{c1::<b>L3-L4</b>}} level will result in:<div>- weakness of {{c2::<u>leg}} extension</u>  and {{c2::<u>thigh}} flexion</u> (action) </div><div>- decreased {{c3::<u>patellar</u>}}<u> reflex</u></div><div>- decreased sensation of {{c4::anterior}} / medial leg</div>	"<div><i>Will affect <u>L4</u> spinal nerve and affect the action of the <b>Femoral</b> nerve</i></div><div><img src=""paste-63952063037441.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div><div><img src=""paste-691584223936513.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::07_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::12_Signs_of_lumbosacral_radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#NBME::Form_22 #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::08_Lumbar_Radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v7 #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

<div><u>Fast twitch</u> muscle fibers are {{c1::white::(color)}} due to {{c2::decreased}} myoglobin concentration</div>	"<img src=""paste-393702472155137.jpg"" />"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::02_Muscle_Anatomy_&_Contraction #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
<b>Slow twitch</b> muscle fibers perform <u>more</u> {{c1::oxidative phosphorylation}} due to increased {{c2::mitochondria}}	- thus more capable of maintain sustained contractions over longer periods of time without fatiguing than <b>fast twitch</b>										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::02_Muscle_Anatomy_&_Contraction #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
<b>Fast twitch</b> muscle fibers perform <u>less</u> {{c1::oxidative phosphorylation}} due to decreased {{c2::mitochondria}}	- thus better suited for short contractions of high intensity<div>- to make up for this, these muscle fibers have more glycogen </div>										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::02_Muscle_Anatomy_&_Contraction #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Type {{c1::II}} muscle fibers are also known as <u>fast twitch</u> muscle fibers	"<div><div>*split into type IIa and IIb</div></div><div>- IIa is intermediate contraction strength and does aerobic and anaerobic metabolism</div><div>- <b>IIb</b> is highest contraction strength and does <b>anaerobic only</b></div><div><br /></div><div>examples include deltoids, biceps brachii, latissimus dorsi, pectoralis major</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-393702472155137.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::02_Muscle_Anatomy_&_Contraction #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
<b>Type {{c1::I}} </b>muscle fibers are increased in proportion after <u>endurance training</u>	"These are known as <b>slow-twitch </b>muscle fibers and are for <u>postural control</u> and <u>walking</u><br /><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-393698177187841.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::02_Muscle_Anatomy_&_Contraction #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Type {{c1::II}} muscle fibers are increased in proportion after <u>weight/resistance training</u> and <u>sprinting</u>	"- These are known as <b>fast-twitch </b>muscle fibers and are better at carrying out <u>power activities</u><br /><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-393698177187841.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::01_Skeletal_Muscle #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::15_Types_of_muscle_fibers #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::02_Muscle_Anatomy_&_Contraction #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGedit #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Bones of the {{c1::axial}} skeleton, {{c2::appendicular}} skeleton, and {{c3::base of the skull}} are formed via {{c4::<b>endochondral}} ossification</b>.						"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"					"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
In {{c1::endochondral}} ossification, a {{c2::hyaline cartilage::specific}} model is first made by chondrocytes. 						"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"					"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v7::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v9::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::700 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
In {{c1::endochondral}} ossification, osteoblasts and osteoclasts replace cartilage with a matrix of {{c2::woven}} bone and then remodel to {{c3::lamellar}} bone.	<div>Uses cartilaginous model compared to membronous forms woven bone directly<br><br>This occurs at the diaphysis (primary center ossification) and the epiphyses (secondary centers of ossification).</div><div>Blasts=lay down; Clasts=resorb</div><div><br></div><div><b>woven</b>=random fiber orientation,  <b>lamellar</b>=parallel fibers arranged in layers (each layer is perpendicular to the adjacent lamellae)</div>					"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"					"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v7::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v9::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
In adults, woven bone is seen {{c1::after fractures}} and in {{c2::Paget disease}}.		Paget disease disrupts the replacement of old bone tissue with new bone tissue.				"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"					"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
{{c1::Endochondral}} ossification is defective in {{c2::achondroplasia}}.	<div>This is why people with achondroplasia have short limbs (affects appendicular skeleton) but normal sized head.</div>					"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"	"<br/><img src=""Achondroplasia - OG  copy.jpg""><img src=""Achondroplasia - OG .jpg""><br>"				"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::01_Biochem::04_Genetics::05_Autosomal_dominant_diseases #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::08_Achondroplasia #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v9::#Pixorize::02_Biochem::05_Autosomal_Dominant::04_Achondroplasia #AK_Step1_v9::#SketchyPath::12_MSK_&_Derm::01_Bone::05_Congenital_Bone_Disorders:_Osteogenesis_Imperfecta,_Achondroplasia_&_Osteopetrosis #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::700 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
Bones of the {{c2::calvarium}}, {{c3::facial bones}}, and {{c4::clavicle}} are formed via {{c1::membranous}} ossification.	"**aka intramembranous<div>""Flat bones"" (also hip and scapula)</div>"	<b>Calvarium</b>: Comprised of the frontal, occipital and two parietal <b>bones</b>.				"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"					"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"
In {{c3::membranous}} ossification, {{c1::woven}} bone is formed <b>{{c4::directly, without cartilage::how?}}</b>. It is then later remodeled to {{c2::lamellar}} bone.	<div>As opposed to endochondral ossification, which is formed from a cartilaginous model. </div>					"<img src=""paste-de7de272bb30f5d9ded844b6113a885bfe3dc0e8.jpg"">"					"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::02_Cell_Biology::04_Bone #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::18_Bone_formation #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::03_Physiology::09_MSK::04_Endochondral_&_Intramembranous_Ossification #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::Anatomy"

{{c2::<b>Osteitis Fibrosa Cystica</b>}} is the classic bone disease of {{c1::<b>primary</b> <b>&amp;</b>&nbsp;<b>secondary</b>}}&nbsp;<b>hyper</b><b>parathyroidism</b>							"<div>* due to elevated PTH -&gt; excessive bone resorption</div><img src=""2 HP osteitis fibrosa cystica.jpg"" class=""resizer"" style=""width: 573px;""><img src=""1 HP .jpg"" class=""resizer"" style=""width: 572px;""><img src=""Zoverall picture-bd830c7fd8feb169533f12642e9419642ce7ea5f_1566160514431.JPG"" class=""resizer"" style=""width: 750px;"">"				"#AK_Step1_v9::!FLAG_THESE_CARDS::SketchyPathAdds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::08_Endocrine::04_Pathology::13_Hyperparathyroidism #AK_Step1_v9::#SketchyPath::07_Endocrine::04_Thyroid_&_Parathyroid::05_Parathyroid_Gland_Disorders #AK_Step1_v9::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v9 #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::JBadd #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::AKadds #AK_Step2_v4::#OME::04_Nephrology::02_CKD #AK_Step2_v4::#OME::04_Nephrology::04_Calcium #AK_Step2_v4::#Resources::IM::ome::renal::calcium #AK_Step2_v4::#Resources::IM::ome::renal::ckd"
The {{c1::synovium}} secretes hyaluronic acid in a synovial joint											"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::OrthoBasicScience"
In <b>Limb Compartment Syndrome</b>, increased tissue pressure within a <u>fascial compartment</u> of a limb <i>exceeds</i> the perfusion pressure, leading to <b>venous</b> {{c1::<b>outflow obstruction</b>}} and {{c2::<b>arteriolar</b>}} <b>collapse</b>	- This leads to <b>anoxia and necrosis</b>, which results in pain and decreased peripheral sensation<div><br /></div><div><div>- irreversible tissue damage occurs between 6-8 hours after onset<br /><div><br /></div></div></div>										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Limb compartment syndrome</b> is often caused by <u>traumatic injury</u> due to:<div><br></div><div>- significant {{c1::long bone}} fractures (both bone)</div><div>- {{c2::reperfusion}} injury</div><div>- animal {{c3::venoms}}</div>	- Typically, <b>tibial</b> or forearm fractures <div><br /><div>- reperfusion injury can be secondary to an <u>arterial thrombosis</u> or <u>crush injury</u></div><div><br /><div><div>- constrictive dressing (splints, casts, or poor surgical positioning can also cause)</div></div></div></div><div><br /></div><div>other injuries include thermal injuries and non traumatic causes (bleeding disorders and vascular disease)</div>										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
As <u>limb compartment syndrome</u> progresses, patients can develop {{c1::motor}} deficits	- this is a late sign of ireversible muscle and nerve damage										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Trochanteric bursitis</b> results from inflammation of the <u>trochanteric bursa</u> and <u>gluteus medius tendon</u> located lateral to the {{c1::greater trochanter}} of the femur<div><br /></div>	"- typically due to <u>gluteus medius overuse</u><div><u><br /></u><div>- treat with NSAIDs, heat, stretching</div><div><br /><div><img src=""paste-33071248179201 (1)-05deb97d4298f37902b014173a84bec8598de825.jpg"" /></div><div><br /></div></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Trochanteric bursitis</b> often occurs because the {{c1::<u>gluteus medius</u>}} is overcompensating to stabilize the hip	"<div>- gluteus medius tendon is constantly rubbing against the trochanteric bursa, causing both to become inflamed</div><div><br></div><div>- often seen in patients who run on <u>banked surfaces</u>;  can be due to instability from a <b>labral</b> <b>tear</b> or <b>ligament</b> <b>injury</b></div><div><b><br></b></div><div><img src=""paste-35716948033537.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div><div><br></div><div><img src=""paste-61138859458561-5b85969a214046c2484f254b5eec69edd152beb7.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
