<b>De Quervain Tenosynovitis </b>presents with <u>pain</u> over the {{c1::radial}} side of the wrist	"- specifically over the <b>radial styloid</b>; pain radiates proximally to the forearm, and distally to thumb<br /><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-126220498894849.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
In <b>De Quervain Tenosynovitis</b>, there is <i>stenosing </i>tenosynovial <u>inflammation</u> of the <u>first</u> dorsal compartment tendons that run over the {{c3::<b>radial styloid</b>}} <b>process</b>, these being the tendons of the:<div><br /></div><div>- {{c1::<b>Abductor Pollicis Longus</b>}}</div><div>- {{c2::<b>Extensor Pollicis Brevis</b>}}</div>	"- The wrist has 6 dorsal compartments ordered from <u>radial to ulnar</u>, these <b>deep extensor tendons</b> travel through the most radial compartment marked <b>(#1</b>)<br><div><br></div><div>-  <b>Abductor Pollicis Longus</b> is responsible for abducting the thumb at the wrist, while the <b>Extensor Pollicis Brevis</b> extends and abducts the thumb</div><div><img class=""resizer"" src=""paste-127289945751553.jpg"" style=""width: 652px;""><img class=""resizer"" src=""paste-95197950115841.jpg"" style=""font-size: 1rem; width: 175px;""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"

What is the most common <u>orthopedic</u> disorder in newborns?<div><br /></div><div>{{c1::Developmental Dysplasia of the Hip}}</div>	"<div>- results in <b>hip instability / dislocation</b></div><div><b><br /></b></div><div><div><img src=""paste-461816358502401.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Developmental Dysplasia of the Hip </b>results from abnormal {{c1::acetabulum}} development in newborns	"<div>- this can precipitate via various methods of displacement (dislocation, subluxation, dysplasia) of the <b>proximal femur</b> from the <b>acetabulum</b></div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-462954524835841.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
Abnormal prenatal development of the <u>acetabulum</u> is caused by excessive {{c1::uterine packing (uterine constraint)}} 	"<div>- This causes excessive stretching of the posterior hip capsule and adductor muscle contractures; thus DDH is a <b>deformation</b></div><div><br /></div><div>- excessive uterine packing describes when fetus is squished (""<u>intrauterine malposition</u>"") too tightly within the uterus (this mechanism can also cause c<b>ongenital torticollis</b>)</div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::08_Actions_of_hip_muscles #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
The <b>Barlow </b>and <b>Ortolani Maneuvers</b> are used to diagnose <b>{{c1::Developmental Dysplasia of the Hip}}</b>	"The Barlow maneuver is performed first to test dislocatability, then followed by the Ortolani maneuver to confirm<div><br></div><div><img src=""paste-479309760299009.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
A <b>Barlow Maneuver </b>is <b>positive</b> if the hip dislocates <b>{{c1::posteriorly}} </b>when pressure is applied to {{c2::<u>adducted</u>}} and {{c2::<u>flexed</u>}} hips	"<div>- thus this is a test of <u>dislocation</u></div>- the <b>Ortolani</b> test is then used to confirm the positive finding<div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-479309760299009.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
"An <b>Ortolani Maneuver </b>is <b>positive</b> if a soft ""<b>clunk</b>"" sound is produced when <u>anterior</u> pressure is applied on the {{c2::<u>greater trochanters</u>}} when the hips are placed in an {{c2::abducted}} position"	"<div>- thus this is a test of <u>reduction</u></div><div>- this confirms the positive finding of a Barlow test</div><div> </div><div><img src=""paste-479309760299009.jpg""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::04_Common_hip_&_knee_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Boxer's Fractures </b>are caused by a fracture of the <b>metacarpal</b> {{c1::neck}} 	"<div>- there will be <b>tenderness to palpation</b> over the affected metacarpal neck; ecchymosis can be seen on the palmar side</div><div><br /></div><div><b><img src=""paste-86655260164097.jpg"" /></b></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::02_Wrist_&_Hand_Injuries #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<div>In the <u>elbow</u> there are three joints, these being:</div><div><br /></div><div><b>- {{c1::Radiocapitellar}} Joint</b></div><div><b>- Ulnohumeral Joint</b></div><div><b>- {{c2::Proximal Radioulnar}} Joint</b></div>	"- <u>Radiocapitellar</u> is where the radial head articulates with the humeral capitellum<div><br /><div>- <u>Ulnohumeral</u> is where the trochlear notch of the ulna articulates with the humeral trochlea</div><div><br /></div><div>- <u>Proximal radioulnar</u> is where the radial head articulates with the radial notch of the ulna</div><div><br /></div><div><div><img src=""paste-32770600468481-a0c69e28f3b68b5deaae86dc9e562ee20ec6b298.jpg"" /></div></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::01_Overuse_injuries_of_the_elbow #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
The <b>proximal radioulnar joint</b> is stabilized by the {{c1::annular ligament}}	"<div>- this ligament originates from the ulna, allowing it to anchor the radial head against it</div><div><br></div>- This ligament becomes trapped in the radiocapitellar joint in <b>Radial Head Subluxation</b><br><div><br></div><div><img src=""paste-72816875536385.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::01_Overuse_injuries_of_the_elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Radial Head Subluxation</b> is caused when there is <i>subluxation</i> of the radial head relative to the {{c1::radiocapitellar}} joint	"- This is due to <b>excess longitudinal traction</b> on a childs arm (vigorous pulling)<br /><div><br /></div><div>- this is <b>treated</b> by <u>reduction</u> (popping it back into place)</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-69599945031681.jpg"" /></div><div><br /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
"<b>Radial Head Subluxation </b>is also known by the name ""{{c1::Nursemaids Elbow}}"""	"- Remember this is due to <b>excess longitudinal traction</b> on a childs arm (<b>vigorous pulling</b>), so imagine an angry nursemaid trying to get a child to come along<div><br /></div><div>- conversely, imagine a scared mother trying to save her child from walking out in the street!</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-69599945031681.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#AMBOSS::Musculoskeletal #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::02_Shoulder_&_Elbow #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
Subluxation of the <u>radial head</u> allows the <b>annular ligament</b> to slip over and into the {{c1::radiocapitellar joint}}	"- Thus this will cause a patient to feel <b>Lateral Elbow Pain</b><div><b><br /></b><div><img src=""paste-69599945031681.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Radial Head Subluxation</b> impinges the <b>{{c1::deep radial}} nerve, </b>causing weakness of <i>most</i> of the <u>posterior forearm {{c2::extensors}}</u>	"<div>- thus patients will hold <b>elbow </b>in <b>slight flexion</b> and their <b>forearm</b> will be <b>pronated</b></div><div><br></div>- remember at the cubital fossa, the radial nerve splits into deep (wrist / finger motor) and superficial (wrist / finger sensory)<div><br></div><div><img src=""paste-36515811950593.jpg"" class=""""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::MedBullets #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::MedBullets #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
Do patients with <b>Radial Head Subluxation </b>present with <u>Wrist Drop</u>?<div><br /></div><div>{{c1::No}}</div>	"- the radial nerve gives off branches to the <b>extensor carpi radialis longus</b> between the radial groove and the cubital fossa; thus patients maintain some extension at the wrist<div><br /></div><div>- thus patients with spiral groove fractures will have wrist drop but radial head subluxation will not<br /><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-36515811950593.jpg"" /></div></div><div><img src=""paste-241407226806273.jpg"" /></div><div><br /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
Do patients with <b>Radial Head Subluxation</b> present with sensory loss over the <u>dorsal hand / wrist</u>?<div><br /></div><div>{{c1::No}}</div>	"<b>- Superficial branch of radial nerve</b> is not impacted by this (distribution shown in picture below), only <u>deep branch of radial nerve</u> (motor only)<br /><div><br /></div><div>- superficial branch palsies are seen in <b>forearm lacerations</b></div><div><b><br /></b></div><div><img src=""paste-37701222924289-01a2b7e543590ee907910853a1cc4c3994aa5e8e.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::03_Radial_&_Long_Thoracic_Nerves #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Radial head subluxation</b> is the most common elbow injury in those under {{c1::5}} years of age	"<div>- the <b>distal attachment</b> of the annular ligament is weaker in children than adults</div><div><br /></div><div>- however, older children usually point to dorsal aspect of proximal forearm vs lateral elbow in a younger child (don't mistake for buckle fracture of proximal radius)</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-32770600468481-a0c69e28f3b68b5deaae86dc9e562ee20ec6b298.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#AMBOSS::Musculoskeletal #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::05_Elbow_&_Wrist #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Osgood-Schlatter Disease</b> is commonly seen in patients after their {{c1::pubertal growth spurt}}	- During pubertal growth spurt the rapid growth of the apophysis exceeds ability for muscle tendon unit to stretch (needed for accommodation of repeated force on tendon insertion point)<div><br></div><div>- thus seen in boys 12-15, girls 8-12</div>										"#AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::01_Knee #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds bab::musculoskeletal::anatomyortho::knee"
An <u>apophysis</u> is a {{c1::secondary ossification center}} which serves as an attachment for a tendon	"- repeated stress on tendon during a time of rapid bone growth (Ex puberty) can result in inflammation on the apophysis<div><br><div>- <b>Osgood-Schlatter</b> = pull of<u> patellar tendon</u> on <b>Tibial Tuberosity</b> causing inflammation</div><div><b><img src=""paste-22308060135425 (1).jpg""></b></div><div><b><br></b></div><div>- <b>Sinding-Larsen-Johansson</b> = pull of <u>patellar tendon</u> on <b>inferior pole of the patella</b> causing inflammation</div></div><div><b><img src=""paste-24223615549441.jpg""></b></div>"	"<img src=""paste-4242cdecbe46cbfc37e33bf1260d01f486340d92.jpg"" draggable=""false"">"									"#AK_Step1_v7::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::01_Knee #AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#B&B::14_MSK::01_Anatomy_&_Orthopedics::01_Knee #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::10_Other_Knee_&_Leg_Conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
"<u>Legg-Calve-Perthes (LCP)</u><b style=""font-weight: bold; ""> </b>and <u>Slipped Capital Femoral Epiphyses (SCFE)</u><b> </b>are both causes of {{c1::Avascular Necrosis of the Femoral Head}}"	"This is a very painful infarction of the head of the femur, caused by some process that impairs the <u>medial circumflex femoral artery</u> from properly working<div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-121075128074241.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
In <b>Legg-Calve-Perthes</b>, there is an {{c1::idiopathic}} mechanism of obstruction of blood to the femoral head	- thought to be related to factors that disrupt blood supply to the femoral head (coagulation abnormalities, trauma)<div><br /></div><div>- if blood supply to the epiphysis is disrupted, the bone dies --&gt; avascular necrosis</div>										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
In <b>Slipped-Capital Femoral Epiphysis</b>, increased {{c1::axial}} force on the femoral head results in the femoral <u>{{c2::epiphysis}}</u> displacing relative to femoral neck	"<div>- Thus <u>obese</u> children have an increased axial force (due to their weight) applied to a unstable growth plate; slippage impacts MCFA</div><div><br></div>- On imaging, this looks like a scoop of ice cream slipping off of a cone<div><br></div><div><img class=""resizer"" src=""paste-57634166145025.jpg"" style=""width: 583px;""></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
In <b>Legg-Calve-Perthes</b>, will you<i> often</i> observe pathology on the <u>initial</u> x-ray?<div><br /></div><div>{{c1::No}}</div>	"- Plain radiographic changes are delayed <u>6 weeks or more</u> from clinical onset, and since clinical symptoms will elicit the initial x-ray being taken, these are usually not seen<br /><div><br /></div><div>- However, a distinguishing feature from a SCFE is that LCP patients will have <b>increased width of the femoral neck</b></div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-55078660603905.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
In <b>Slipped Capital Femoral Epiphysis</b> will you<i> often</i> observe pathology on the <u>initial</u> x-ray?<div><br /></div><div>{{c1::Yes}}</div>	"<div>Here you can see the ""Ice-cream scoop slide"" appearance of the femoral head</div><div><br /><div><img src=""paste-57634166145025.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
<b>Developmental Dysplasia of the Hip</b> can be treated with a <u>Pavlik Harness</u>, which keeps the hips {{c1::flexed}} and {{c1::abducted}}	"<div><div>- if this fails, then open or closed reduction with spica casting is appropriate (depending on the age and severity of disease)</div></div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-12949326397441.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::06_Childhood_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::#Physeo::02_Anatomy::08_MSK::07_Hips #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
"<b>De Quervain Tenosynovitis</b> is now most commonly associated with patients who are {{c1::<u>new mothers</u> (""Mother's Wrist"")}} and patients who type on {{c2::<u>small handheld devices</u> (""Texting Thumb"")}}"	"- Seen in new mothers / daycare workers due to repetitive lifting of the baby<div><br></div><div>- also seen in factory workers / racquet sport players<br><div><br></div><div><img src=""paste-95825015341057.jpg"" draggable=""false""></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::05_Common_MSK_conditions #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^FA_Updates::FA2018 #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
A fracture of the {{c1::middle third::what section?}} c<b>lavicle</b> is the most common <u>clavicle fracture</u>	"clavicle fractures are based on location (medial third, middle third, and lateral third)<div><br /></div><div>typically, a middle third fracture will result in the <b>SCM</b> pulling the medial fragment <u>posterosuperiorly</u> and the <b>pectoralis minor</b> pulling the lateral fragment <u>inferomedially</u><br /><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-74040941215745.jpg"" /></div><div><br /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::03_Clavicle_fractures #AK_Step1_v9::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v6 #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
What fracture occurs most commonly from a direct fall on the shoulder?<div><br /></div><div>{{c1::Clavicle fracture}}</div>	This is in both adults and children; can also be caused by a direct blow to the lateral aspect of the shoulder										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::02_Pathology::03_Clavicle_fractures #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"

<b>Piriformis syndrome</b> is caused by hypertrophy / muscle injury to the piriformis compressing structures traveling through the {{c1::greater sciatic foramen}}	"- <b>Sciatic nerve</b> (which is impinged), internal pudendal vessels, and and inferior gluteal vessels cross below the piriformis<div><br /></div><div>- structures that cross above the piriformis include superior gluteal nerve and gluteal vessels</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-25327422144513-ba165fa952c8e98b9f11f247c6d23a236482a921.jpg"" /></div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-26113401159681 (1).jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::12_Signs_of_lumbosacral_radiculopathy #AK_Step1_v9::#UWorld::11727 #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"

The <b>Achilles tendon</b> is formed from the {{c1::soleus}} and {{c1::gastrocnemius}} muscles and acts in {{c2::plantar flexion}}	"this inserts on the <b>posterior calcaneus</b><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-180474525777921.jpg"" /></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::11_Ankle_sprains #AK_Step1_v9::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v1-v5 #AK_Step1_v9::$AnKingUpdates::$Errata::v6 #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"

Before the<b> median nerve</b> enters the wrist / hand within the {{c2::<u>flexor retinaculum</u>}}, it travels through the forearm between the <u>{{c1::flexor digitorum superficialis}}</u> and the <u>{{c1::flexor digitorum profundus}}</u>	"<div>The median nerve will enter the carpal tunnel with the tendons of these muscles</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-59188944306177.jpg"" /></div><div><i><img src=""paste-112429358907393.jpg"" /></i></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
The<b> median nerve</b> gains access to the forearm in the medial aspect of the <u>{{c2::antecubital}} fossa</u> and then immediately courses between the humeral / ulnar heads of the <b>{{c1::pronator teres}}</b> muscle	"ulnar head orginates at coronoid process<br>humeral head orginates at medial supracondylar ridge<br><img src=""paste-59193239273473.jpg"">"										"#AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v7::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v7::Musculoskeletal::BGadds #AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"
The {{c3::<b>dorsal</b> <b>scapular</b>}} <b>nerve </b>provides motor innervation to the <u>rhomboids</u> ({{c1::<u>retract</u>}}<u> the scapula</u>) and the <u>levator scapulae</u> muscles ({{c2::<u>elevate</u>}} <u>the scapula</u>)	"**C3-C5<div>- Dorsal scapular nerve entrapment syndrome is a cause of neck and shoulder pain, presenting with a dull ache along the medial scapula<div><br /></div><div>- may be injured in the neck <u>at the scalene </u>or in the thoracic back at the level of the rhomboids</div><div><br /></div><div><img src=""paste-465041878941697.jpg"" /></div></div>"										"#AK_Step1_v9::#FirstAid::11_MSK,_Skin,_&_Connective::01_Anatomy_&_Physiology::03_Upper_extremity_nerves #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGadd #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::BGnonessentials #AK_Step1_v9::^EXPN::Uworld #AK_Step1_v9::Musculoskeletal::BGadds"

<b>Long bones</b> (femur, humerus, tibia etc) consists of diaphysis which is {{c1::thick}} cortical bone surrounding a central canal of {{c1::cancellous/trabecular}} bone, the metaphysis which is {{c2::thin}} cortical bones surrounding <b>loose </b>{{c2::trabecular/cancellous}} bone, and the epiphysis which is the end of the bone forming the articular surface which contains the {{c3::physis}} and {{c3::subchondral}} region under the articular cartilage	"<img src=""paste-ed1ba57db616cefc863ea416b6d20636cf49f25b.jpg"">"										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
<b>Flat bones</b> (e.g. skull, pelvis, scapula) have varied structure of either <b>purely&nbsp;</b>{{c1::cortical}}&nbsp;<b>bone </b>or {{c1::cortical}}&nbsp;<b>bone</b> with a <b>thin central&nbsp;</b>{{c1::trabecular}}&nbsp;<b>region</b>											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
<b>Cortical </b>bone makes up 80% of skeleton and is characterized by a <b>slow turnover rate</b> and {{c1::high}} <b><u>Young's modulus</u></b>	"<img src=""paste-f2e98500a0e2b62476e51de6138e90ad319e2841.jpg"">"										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
{{c1::Cancellous}}&nbsp;<b>bone </b>is also known as {{c2::<b>spongy</b>}} or {{c2::trabecular}} bone and is characterized by a {{c1::high}} <b>turnover rate </b>with a {{c1::lower}} <b>Young's modulus </b>and <b>more elastic</b>	"<img src=""paste-f3bdd008bb0d6119d0ebce2b4713d4c772e69d2c.jpg"">"										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
{{c1::Cancellous}} bone is 30-90% porous and <b>contains bone marrow&nbsp;</b>											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
{{c1::Woven}} bone is <b>immature </b>or <b>pathologic </b>bone that is random and not stress oriented											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
Compared to lamellar bone, <b>woven bone </b>has more {{c1::osteocytes}} per unit of volume, {{c1::higher}}&nbsp;<b>rate of turnover,&nbsp;</b>and are more weaker and flexible than lamellar bone											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
{{c1::Lamellar}}<b>&nbsp;bone</b> is a secondary bone created by <b><u>remodeling</u></b> woven bone and is <b>organized </b>and <b>stress oriented</b>	stronger and less flexible than woven bone										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::TypesOfBone
"<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">The <b>Young's modulus (E)</b> is a property of the material that tells us how easily it can stretch and deform and is defined as&nbsp;</span><b>the ratio of tensile</b> {{c1::stress (σ)}}<b>&nbsp;to tensile&nbsp;</b>{{c1::strain (ε)}}<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">.&nbsp;</span>"											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
"In regard to <b>Young's modulus</b>,&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);""><b><u>stress</u></b> is the amount of&nbsp;</span>{{c1::force}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">applied per&nbsp;</span>{{c1::unit area (σ = F/A)}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">and <b><u>strain </u></b>is extension per unit length (ε = dl/l).</span>"											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
<div><div><b><u>cFOS </u></b>is involved in {{c1::osteoclast}} differentiation</div></div>											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
In regards to bone metabolism, IL-{{c1::1}} stimualtes <b>osteoclastic bone resorption</b>.											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
"{{c1::Osteocalcin}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(51, 51, 51);"">is the most specific marker of the <b>osteoblast phenotype </b>and is expressed only in <b><u>mature osteoblasts</u></b></span>"											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
<b>Osteoblasts </b>function to form bone by producing non-mineralized matrix made of {{c1::alkaline phosphatase}}, <b>type 1 collagen</b>, {{c1::osteonectin}} and <b>osteocalcin</b>											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
{{c1::<b>Estrogen</b>}} <b>inhibits bone resorption </b>and stimulates bone production by {{c2::<b>inhibiting</b>}} adenylyl cyclase	thus loss of estrogen in menopause increases resorption and predisposes to osteoporosis										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
{{c1::Glucocorticoids::medication}} inhibit collagen and bone matrix production											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
{{c1::<b>Prostaglandins</b>}} <b>stimulate bone resorption </b>by {{c2::activating}} adenylyl cyclase											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
{{c1::Osteoblasts}} are derived from <b>undifferentiated mesenchymal </b>cells which <b>differentiate </b>into {{c2::osteoprogenitor}} cells											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
"{{c2::Osteoclasts&nbsp;}}<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">originate from</span><span style=""color: rgb(83, 114, 166);"">&nbsp;</span>{{c1::myeloid}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(83, 114, 166);"">hematopoietic cell</span><span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">s from monocyte/macrophage cell lineage</span>"											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
<b>Osteoclasts </b>conduct bone resorption at {{c1::Howship's lacunae}} which are areas where <b>ruffled borders </b>meet the bone surface											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
<b><u>Osteoclasts</u></b> secrete {{c1::tartrate resistant acid phosphatase}} which utilizes {{c2::carbonic anhydrase II}} to {{c2::lower}} the pH and <b>increase </b>the <b>solubility </b>of hydroxyapatite crystals&nbsp;											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
Deficiency of {{c1::carbonic anhydrase II}} <b><u>prevents </u>bone resorption </b>as there will be <b><u>loss</u></b> of the acidic environment&nbsp;	normall CAII creates H+ to acidify and promote resorption										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
<b>Osteoclasts </b>are involved in <b>proteolytic digestion </b>of organic matrix of bone and {{c1::cathepsin K}} is one of the major enzymes used at the ruffled border											OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
{{c1::Osteocytes}} are <b>former</b>&nbsp;<b>osteoblasts </b>trapped in the matrix they produced accounting for 90% of cells in the mature skeleton	"<img src=""paste-40e333d0f3a69f3cab349fa085366137a5f15156.jpg"">"										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells
{{c1::Osteocytes}} make up 90-95% of cells in bone tissue and function to <b>maintain bone and cellular matrix</b> through <b>regulating calcium </b>and <b>phosphate </b>concentrations	"osteoblasts and osteoclasts only make up about 5%<br><img src=""paste-40e333d0f3a69f3cab349fa085366137a5f15156.jpg"">"										OB::BasicScience::MSKScience::BoneCells

What <b>Position</b> should a <b>Shoulder</b> be <b>Fused </b>in?<br><br>{{c1::20<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;Forward Flexion, 20<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;ABduction, 40<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;Internal Rotation}}	Not typically done anymore, sometimes a pimp question / test answer										"#Ortho::PP::ShoulderGirdle::GeneralAnatomy"
The {{c1::Anterior}} <b>bundle</b> of the <b>MCL</b> of the <b>Elbow</b> is the <b>Strongest</b> and <b>attaches</b> to the {{c1::Sublime Tubercle of the Ulna (Medial Coronoid Process)}}	"Primary&nbsp;<b>restraint</b>&nbsp;to&nbsp;<b>Valgus</b>&nbsp;motion<br><img class=""resizer"" src=""elbow-ligaments.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::PP::Elbow::GeneralAnatomy"
What <b>Position</b> should an <b>Elbow</b> be fused in when <b>Unilateral</b>?<br><br>{{c1::90<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;Flexion}}											"#Ortho::PP::Elbow::GeneralAnatomy"
What <b>Position</b> should <b>Elbows</b> be fused in when <b>Bilateral</b>? <b>Why</b>?<br><br>{{c1::110<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;Flexion to <u>reach mouth</u>, and 65<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;Flexion for <u>Perianal Hygiene</u>}}											"#Ortho::PP::Elbow::GeneralAnatomy"
Applying {{c1::traction}} allows constant controlled force for initial stabilization of<b>&nbsp;</b>{{c2::long bone}}<b>&nbsp;fractures </b>and <b>aids in&nbsp;</b>{{c2::<b>reduction</b>}} during operative procedures.	"<img src=""paste-c5cbbc0444f4a5e240b7eab90307e57f0eabc6a1.jpg"">"										
{{c1::Skin (Buck's)}} <b>traction </b>only allows for limited force, typically not to exceed {{c2::10}} lbs, and is an option for <b><u>temporary comfort </u></b>in hip fractures and children fractures	"keep an eye out for soft tissue problems that may occur; max of 7-10 lbs used and not as powerful as skeletal traction<br><img src=""paste-a7822ddc3b52612e23d04f3ac43868cc83eeedd4.jpg"">"										
<b>Skeletal traction</b> is more powerful, with greater fragment control, than <b>skin traction </b>and permits pull up to {{c1::15}}% to {{c1::20}}% of body weight for the lower extremity.&nbsp;	starting around 15 lbs or 10% of body weight is a good start!<br><br>It requires local anesthesia for pin insertion if the patient is awake										
"{{c1::Skeletal Traction}} is the preferred method of <b>temporizing long bone fractures</b>, {{c2::pelvic}}, and {{c2::acetabular}}&nbsp;<b>fractures </b>until operative
treatment can be performed."											
Choice of pin in <b>skeletal traction</b> can be <b><u>thin </u></b>{{c1::Kirschner (K-wire)}} or {{c1::Steinmann pin}}	"K-wire is more difficult to insert with a hand drill and requires a tension traction bow
(Kirschner)."										
The <b>Steinmann pin </b>may be either {{c1::smooth}} or {{c1::threaded}}	"A smooth pin is stronger but can slide through bone. A threaded pin is&nbsp;<b>weaker&nbsp;</b>and bends more easily with increasing weights, but it will not slide and will advance more easily during insertion.<br><br>In general, the largest pin available (5 to 6 mm) is chosen, especially if a threaded pin is selected.<br><br><img src=""paste-61c694fc64ee3009f58ebe0faf6092f2bf71fc7b.jpg"">"										
A {{c1::smooth}} pin is <b>stronger </b>but {{c2::does}}&nbsp;<b>slide through bone</b>. A {{c1::threaded}} pin is&nbsp;<b>weaker&nbsp;</b>and <b>bends more easily</b> with increasing weights, but {{c2::does not}} <b>slide </b>and will <b>advance more easily</b> during insertion.	"In general, the largest pin available (5 to 6 mm) is chosen, especially if a <b>threaded </b>pin is selected.<br><br><img src=""paste-61c694fc64ee3009f58ebe0faf6092f2bf71fc7b.jpg"">"										
When conducting skeletal traction, more <b><u>proximal </u></b>traction is generally better than distal traction ({{c1::femoral}} &gt; {{c1::tibial}} &gt; {{c1::calcaneal}})	"olecranon skeletal traction rarely used anymore<br><img src=""paste-beaaff69487289a15c4bb5c0f6aa3c88bcd6725b.jpg"">"										
When placing a Distal femur traction pin, goal is {{c1::1-2}} fingerbreadths <b>proximal </b>from the {{c1::superior patella pole}}	"Too proximal is bad, because it is close to Hunter’s (adductor) canal and close to the <b>neurovascular </b>bundle<br>Also the traction bow won’t fit around the knee<br>Avoid being too distal, you don’t want to pass through the intercondylar notch<br><img src=""paste-7f3dc187488b26e1bad371cb2ca6210ca935311c.jpg"">"										
When placing distal femur traction pins being <b>too proximal</b> is bad, because it is close to {{c1::Hunter’s (adductor) canal}} which allows for passage of the&nbsp;<b>neurovascular </b>bundle	"<div>When placing distal femur traction pins being&nbsp;<b>too&nbsp;distal&nbsp;</b>is bad, because you&nbsp;don’t want to pass through the intercondylar notch<img src=""paste-7f3dc187488b26e1bad371cb2ca6210ca935311c.jpg""><br></div>"										
When placing distal femur traction pins being&nbsp;<b>too&nbsp;distal&nbsp;</b>is bad, because you&nbsp;don’t want to pass through the {{c1::intercondylar notch}}	"<img src=""paste-7f3dc187488b26e1bad371cb2ca6210ca935311c.jpg"">"										
{{c2::Adductor (Hunter's) Canal}}&nbsp;is passageway for structures moving between the {{c1::anterior thigh}} and {{c1::posterior leg}}. It transmits the {{c4::femoral artery}}, {{c4::femoral vein (posterior to the artery)}}, nerve to the {{c3::vastus medialis}} and the {{c3::saphenous nerve (the largest cutaneous branch of the femoral nerve.)}}	"<img src=""paste-67ab2e76c563f6c9e14513c56ddddff6ec6aaef2.jpg"">"										
The <b>Adductor (Hunter's) canal</b> runs from the <b>apex of the femoral triangle</b> to the adductor hiatus is bordered <b>anteromedially </b>be the {{c1::sartorius}}, <b>laterally </b>by the {{c2::vastus medalis}}, and <b>posterior </b>by the {{c3::adductor longus}} and {{c3::adductor magnus}}	"<img src=""paste-67ab2e76c563f6c9e14513c56ddddff6ec6aaef2.jpg"">"										
"<b>Femoral traction pin</b> is placed from {{c1::medial}} to {{c1::lateral}} (directed away from the neurovascular bundle) at the
{{c2::adductor tubercle}}, slightly <b>proximal to the femoral epicondyle.</b>"	"<img src=""paste-369189dfc376616736d8ca462bac2da0ecd5d36f.jpg"">"										
<b>Tibial traction pin</b> is placed from {{c1::lateral}} to {{c1::medial}} to direct the pin away from the {{c2::common peroneal nerve}}.	The pin is placed 2 cm posterior and 1 cm distal to the tibial tubercle.										
"<b>Calcaneal</b> <b>traction pins </b>are used for irreducible rotational ankle fractures, some pilon fractures, and extremities with
multiple ipsilateral long bone fractures or compromised soft tissues and is placed from {{c1::medial}} to {{c1::lateral}}, directed away from the neurovascular bundle, 2 to 2.5 cm {{c3::posterior}} and {{c3::inferior}} to the {{c2::medial malleolus}}."	"about ¾ of the distance from the tip of the medial mal to the end of the calcaneus.<br><img src=""paste-56618f3170aefc324e6bfc40ed0e5141a0fc1dc3.jpg"">"										
"<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">A&nbsp;</span>{{c1::pilon}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">fracture is&nbsp;</span><b>a type of break of the distal&nbsp;tibia plateau that happens near the ankle</b><span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">. Most of the time, it includes breaks in both the tibia and fibula of the lower leg.</span>"	"<img src=""paste-ac7c1cc743175e7d6bbd8a8f0133d3cc8e12123a.jpg"">"										
"<b><u>Acetabulum of the hip </u></b>can be divided into <b>four quadrants </b>based on a line&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">drawn from <b>ASIS through center of acetabulum</b> which splits it into&nbsp;</span>{{c1::anterior}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">and&nbsp;</span>{{c1::posterior}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">quadrants. Another perpendicular line bisects the previous line to form&nbsp;</span>{{c1::superior}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">and&nbsp;</span>{{c1::inferior}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">quadrants</span>"	"<img src=""paste-ba1b929bb6c4b7f40c327016b33921496a35d8d2.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
The {{c1::posterior superior}} portion of the <b>acetabulum </b>contains {{c2::superior gluteal}} nerve and vessels and {{c2::sciatic}} nerve. It is the {{c1::safest}} zone for screw placement	"<img src=""paste-46f6e4745ae69792a1be3dc667091b850963cf2c.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
The {{c1::posterior inferior}} portion of the <b>acetabulum&nbsp;</b>contains {{c2::inferior gluteal}} nerve and vessels and {{c2::internal pudendal}} nerve and vessels. It is the {{c1::second safest}} zone for screw placement	"<img src=""paste-46f6e4745ae69792a1be3dc667091b850963cf2c.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
The {{c1::anterior inferior}} portion of the <b>acetabulum </b>contains the {{c2::obuturator}} nerve, artery, and vein. It is {{c3::unsafe}} zone for screw placement.	"<img src=""paste-46f6e4745ae69792a1be3dc667091b850963cf2c.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
The {{c1::anterior superior}} portion of the <b>acetabulum </b>contains the {{c2::external iliac}} artery and vein. It is {{c3::unsafe}} zone for screw placement.&nbsp;	"<img src=""paste-46f6e4745ae69792a1be3dc667091b850963cf2c.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
"<div style=""display: inline !important;"">The {{c1::iliofemoral}} ligament&nbsp;(<b>Y ligament of Bigelow) </b>is the&nbsp;</div>strongest ligament of the hip and runs from the {{c2::AIIS}} to {{c2::intertrochanteric}} line and prevents anterior dislocation / hyperextension"	"<img src=""paste-ca5ee84ecb8706445c4aa665252d164349218c0d.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
"The {{c1::Ischiofemoral}} ligament<b>&nbsp;</b>attaches to the posterior surface of the acetabular rim and labrum and courses circumferentially around the joint to its insertion on the anterior aspect of the femur<font color=""#202124"">&nbsp;and<b> provides posterior support of the hip</b></font>"	"<img src=""paste-043ffe1349e2aaee998bd43b544c01d10ce38bd3.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
The three main ligaments of the hip are the {{c1::iliofemoral}}, {{c1::ischiofemoral}}, and {{c1::pubofemoral}} ligaments	"<img src=""paste-b4a198c86f7aad4e98c2af0f4afb9a5feec6f50a.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
The {{c1::sciatic nerve (peroneal division)}} is the most commonly injured neural structure during a THA	"innervates short head of the biceps;&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(33, 33, 33);"">Sciatic nerve palsy is the most commonly encountered nerve injury associated with THA, accounting for over 90% of all reported nerve injuries.</span><sup><a href=""https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5884042/#bib0010"">2</a></sup><span style=""color: rgb(33, 33, 33);"">&nbsp;The sciatic nerve exits the pelvis via the greater sciatic notch, anterior to the piriformis muscle, and then branches into its common peroneal and tibial divisions.&nbsp;</span>"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
"<span style=""color: rgb(51, 51, 51);"">In a majority of hips, the main blood supply of the femoral head is from the&nbsp;</span>{{c1::medial femoral circumflex artery (MFCA)}} which is a branch of the {{c2::deep femoral artery (profunda femoris)}} which branched from the {{c2::femoral artery off of the external iliac artery::2}}"	"<img src=""paste-a0877f68ddfc9945696681c50159ddf1fd1fe02f.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
Outside of the medial femoral circumflex artery, the {{c1::<b>inferior gluteal artery</b>}} which is a branch of the {{c2::<b>internal iliac artery</b>}} and runs along the border of the {{c3::<b>piriformis</b>}} tendon has been found to be the primary blood supply of the femoral head in up to 17% of patients	"Thus to prevent AVN of the hip, both arteries should be protected during surgery.<br><img src=""paste-87ae0e9a13c17292caed28c0a451672ce2c5ce93.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-2b78392df51b86397de07b0724f62e8a3630cfa2.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::HipAnatomy
In prosthetic design, the concept of {{c1::Femoral rollback}} is the posterior translation of the femur with progressive knee flexion and<b> improves quadricep function</b> and <b>restores range of motion in the knee</b>	"rollback in the native knee is controlled by ACL and PCL<br><img src=""paste-cd14aae12955ff1e90013eb3944204277b961dfb.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
{{c1::PCL retaining (cruciate-retaining)}} and {{c1::PCL substituting/posterior stabilized}} prosthetic designs allow for femoral rollback&nbsp;	"<img src=""paste-cd14aae12955ff1e90013eb3944204277b961dfb.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
In prosthetic design, the concept of {{c1::Constraint}} is&nbsp;the ability of a prosthesis to provide varus-valgus and flexion-extension stability in the face of {{c2::ligamentous laxity}} or bone loss											OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
In the setting of <b>ligamentous laxity </b>or <b>severe bone loss</b>, standard {{c1::cruciate-retaining (PCL-retaining)}} or {{c1::posterior-stabilized (PCL-substituting)}} implants may not provide stability											OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
"{{c2::Cruciate-retaining aka PCL-retaining}} design is a&nbsp;minimally constrained prosthesis that depends on an intact {{c1::PCL}}<span style=""color: rgb(83, 114, 166);"">&nbsp;</span>to provide stability in flexion"	"<img src=""paste-3a457d5e4eae81ec72ecdd3436775520ee9ba309.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
<b>Advantages of&nbsp;</b>{{c2::cruciate-retaining}}<b>&nbsp;prosthetic design</b> is avoidance of {{c1::tibial post-femoral cam}} impingment, more closely resembles normal knee movement, and less distal femur needs to be cut	"<img src=""paste-893f15223523330652a98dfc5e209f972ea3aaf2.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
<b>Disadvantages </b>of {{c1::cruciate-retaining}}&nbsp;prosthetic design is <b>accelerated polyethelene wear </b>due to <b>tight PCL</b> and also may cause<b> loose or ruptured PCL</b> leading to flexion instability and subluxation											OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
"<div style=""display: inline !important;"">In order of least constrained to most constrained Knee implabts</div><div><ul><li><div>{{c1::cruciate-retaining}}</div></li></ul><ul><li><div>{{c1::posterior-stabilized (cruciate-substituting)}}</div></li></ul><ul><li><div>{{c1::varus-valgus constrained (non-hinged CCK (contrained condylar knee))}}</div></li></ul><ul><li><div>{{c1::rotating-hinge}}</div></li></ul></div>"	least to most able to provide varus/valgus and flexion/extension in setting of bone loss and ligamental laxity!										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
{{c1::Posterior stabilized aka PCL-substituting}} design is a more constrained prosthesis that requires <b>resection of PCL </b>and has a femoral cam component that engages with tibial polyethelyne post during flexion&nbsp;	"<img src=""paste-b245dbe4fe69e5e8ed9616a9d16ab152158db0d3.jpg"">&nbsp;<img src=""paste-7493204aca3e99e73bab956e9a6fd41aff840a69.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
<b>Advantages </b>of {{c1::posterior stabilized design}} is <b>easier to balance a knee</b> with absent PCL,<b> more range of motion,</b> and easier surgical exposure											OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
"One disadvantages of <b>posterior stabilized design</b> is {{c1::""cam jump""}} which can occur due to&nbsp;<b>loose flexion gap</b> or <b>hyperextension </b>where the cam rotates over the post and dislocates. Can be treated with closed reduction anterior drawer initially and then revision procedure to address loose flexion gap.&nbsp;"	"<img src=""paste-985a95afd6c990f9ad0a2627580dc3cdb016857f.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
"One <b>disadvantage </b>of {{c3::posterior stabilized}}<b><u>&nbsp;design</u></b> is {{c2::patellar ""clunk"" syndrome}} which is a&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">painful, palpable ""clunk"" at the patellofemoral articulation </span><span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">caused by a fibrous nodule of&nbsp;</span>{{c1::scar tissue}}<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">&nbsp;at the posterior surface of the <b>distal quadriceps tendon/superior patellar pole</b> catching on the <b>box of the femoral component</b> during knee extension</span>"	"treatment is with arthroscopic versus open resection of scar tissue<br><img src=""paste-a4a6e3ecb7ab9e3be33f088c53204aad5afb8225.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis
{{c1::Constrained nonhinged design (CCK)}} is a prosthesis used in patients with <b>LCL or MCL attenuation or deficiency</b> and has a large tibial post and deep femoral box to provide <b>high varus/valgus stability and rotation stability&nbsp;</b>	"constrained condylar knee<br><img src=""paste-447819436b9accc275669dab92782dc780b44d22.jpg""><img src=""paste-e8cee27000f40627f940bb779ac115494b105637.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::TKAProsthesis

<div><b>Femoral Neck Fractures</b>&nbsp;can be classified based on location:<br>–{{c1::subcapital}}<strong>:</strong>&nbsp;upper third of the femoral neck below the femoral head</div><div>–{{c1::transcervical}}<strong>:</strong>&nbsp;middle of the femoral neck</div><div>–{{c1::basicervical}}: base of femoral neck, <b><u>above </u></b>the {{c2::greater}} and {{c2::lesser}} trochanter</div><div>–{{c1::intertrochanteric}}: occurs <b><u>between </u></b>the {{c2::greater}} and {{c2::lesser}} trochanter</div><div>–{{c1::subtrochanteric}}: below the {{c3::lesser}} trochanter, extending {{c3::5}} cm down (below is considered a <b>femoral shaft fracture</b>)</div>	"<img src=""paste-4c9a2f2f4bfc6e008a7a4c2f3372e173c3bde597.jpg""><img src=""paste-2a9439dfeb3318d6eb1dd469e03a1c1253265851.jpg"">"										OB::Trauma::FemNeckFractures

<b><u>Intertrochanteric</u></b> hip fractures are managed based on their inherent stability:<br><div><strong><em>Stable</em></strong>&nbsp;fractures are treated with either {{c1::<b>short or long intramedullary nails</b>}} or {{c1::<b>sliding hip screws</b>}}</div><div><strong><em>Unstable</em></strong>&nbsp;fractures are treated with {{c2::<b>intramedullary nails</b> (<strong>cephalomedullary nail</strong>}}</div>	"<div>AAOS recommends either SHS or CMN for stable fractures; equal outcomes<br>Long vs Short is controversial for CMN<br><img src=""paste-786e09bf26692fbdf45a02722b2e2e72511053fa.jpg""><br>If any one factor is met, the intertrochanteric fracture is unstable, and surgical management changes accordingly.&nbsp;</div><div>4 factors that dictate stability are:</div><ol><li>reverse obliquity: the fracture line is perpendicular to the line drawn from the greater to the lesser trochanter</li><li>Lateral cortex involvement</li><li>Calcar comminution: The calcar is an area of thickened bone that is located deep to the lesser trochanter on the posteromedial aspect of the femoral shaft. This area aids in load distribution and provides mechanical support to the proximal femur.</li><li>Subtrochanteric extension</li></ol><img src=""paste-799fbe3dc0477dd1a853e5f0eaac22b4c1e3616d.jpg""><img src=""paste-ede8fd99bc2302f01925b362a9b21c0a5ec5bbc3.jpg""><img src=""paste-873d0bf09a6dd82808305f078eb53bf526739260.jpg""><br>"										OB::Trauma::FemNeckFractures
<b>Intertrocanteric fractures </b>are based on the following 4 factors that dictate stability:<br><ol><li>{{c4::<b>reverse obliquity</b>}}: the fracture line is {{c4::<b>perpendicular</b>}} to the line drawn from the greater to<b> </b>the lesser trochanter</li><li>{{c3::<b>Lateral cortex</b>}} involvement</li><li><b>Calcar comminution</b>: The calcar is an area of thickened bone that is located deep to the lesser trochanter on the {{c1::posteromedial}} aspect of the femoral shaft. "Diagnosis of rotator cuff tears can be suspected clinically with&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">provocative tests of the supraspinatous, infraspinatous, teres minor and subscapularis, but confirmation requires an&nbsp;</span>{{c1::MRI}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">of the shoulder.&nbsp;</span>"											OB::Shoulder/Elbow::RotatorCuffTears

<b>Rotator cuff tears</b> are classified based on size: small ({{c1::0}}-{{c1::1}} cm), medium ({{c1::1}}-{{c1::3}} cm), large ({{c1::3}}-{{c1::5}} cm) and massive (&gt;{{c1::5}} cm involving 2 or more tendons)	"<img src=""paste-09ae5e23c710e5ae36f83068c9e94e637410bcd5.jpg"">"										OB::Shoulder/Elbow::RotatorCuffTears

{{c1::Neutralization}} plates are used to&nbsp;prevent bending and rotational forces to protect a lag screw fixation.											Ortho::Clerkships
<b>Lag screw by technique</b> utilizes a {{c1::gliding hole}} in the {{c1::near}} cortex to allow for <b>screw threads</b> to bite the <b>far cortex </b>and <b>compress </b>a fracture	"<b>Lag screws can be used by design or technique.&nbsp;</b><br>ex: using a 2.7 mm diameter screw will have a calibrated drill bit create a 2.7 mm hole in the near cortex and then a 2.0 mm hole in the far cortex. A 2.7 mm hole with ""x"" length (determined by depth guage) will pass through the near cortex without engaging and threads will only bite the far cortex allowing fracture to compress"										Ortho::Clerkships
{{c1::Lag screw by design}}&nbsp;varies in presentation but often can be seen with a <b>partially threaded screw </b>and another screw (worm screw) drilled parallel to it allowing for the<b> distal threads to interweave</b> with worm screw and <b>compress the fracture</b>	"<img src=""paste-504def0537d859afb35b852b0398e626addaa1b6.jpg"">"										Ortho::Clerkships

<b><u>Lateral ligament of ankle</u></b> includes the {{c1::<b>anterior talofibular</b> <b>ligament </b>(<b>ATFL</b>)}} (most common injured), {{c2::<b>calcaneofibular</b>}} (2nd most commonly injured), {{c3::<b>posterior talofibular ligament</b>}} (strongest/least likely injured)	"<img src=""paste-929476f4e8eb519700f8697ea98d91263adad108.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures
Syndesmotic ligaments of the ankle stabilize the tibia and fibula and include the {{c1::anterior inferior tibiofibular ligament&nbsp;(AITFL)}}<b>,</b> {{c1::posterior inferior tibiofibular ligament (PITFL)}}<b>,&nbsp;</b>{{c2::inferior transverse ligament&nbsp;(ITL)::runs below the PITFL}}<b>, and&nbsp;</b>{{c3::interosseous ligament (IOL)::distal continuation of IOM}}	"<img src=""paste-700c7833d15d9d7592a17fbf03f269c025d7e443.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures
<b><u>Posterolateral </u>fibular plating</b>&nbsp;encounters the {{c1::fibularis (peroneal)}} tendons and {{c1::sural}} nerve which are at risk for injury	"<img src=""paste-c8c258daa3d5246b50e3e2f07561867664375076.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures Ortho::Approaches
<b>Posteromedial approach </b>to the ankle encounters the {{c1::tibalis posterior}} tendon and the {{c1::posterior tibial bundle (nerve and artery)}} which are at risk for injury	"<div>Tom dick and Harry&nbsp;</div><img src=""paste-85da2b640c367e4f271b15283f7226d504f24106.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures Ortho::Approaches

"{{c2::Maisonneuve}}&nbsp;<strong>fracture</strong><span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">&nbsp;refers to a combination of a fracture of the&nbsp;</span>{{c1::proximal&nbsp;fibula}}<span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">together with an unstable&nbsp;</span>{{c1::ankle}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">injury (widening of the ankle mortise on x-ray)</span>"	"<span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">&nbsp;often comprising ligamentous injury (distal&nbsp;</span><a href=""https://radiopaedia.org/articles/distal-tibiofibular-syndesmosis?lang=us"">tibiofibular syndesmosis</a><span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">,&nbsp;</span><a href=""https://radiopaedia.org/articles/deltoid-ligament-of-the-ankle-1?lang=us"">deltoid ligament</a><span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);"">) and/or fracture of the medial malleolus<br></span><img src=""paste-02340d039af5be765a28ea35f0d6ec571c2ea7f3.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(61, 61, 61);""><br></span>"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures

"<div><b>Vertical medial Mal&nbsp;</b></div><b>causes avulsion of lateral mal and oblique fracture of the medial mal</b><br><img src=""paste-46eace9ad68347c048ce23324b2d5df288f32a16.jpg""><img src=""paste-d5ed71a5e3f68f4007bc79c6d8dd1fd0f6fec8f8.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures

"Causes oblique fracture of the fibula; transverse fracture of the medial mal<br><img src=""paste-0dda7126267880b820f6e150266c97c945c7d393.jpg""><img src=""paste-f129d6aec0c65241851d4b5a30e85a1c2f2fd5e1.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures

"leads to fibula fracture above syndesmosis and avulsion of the medial and posterior mal<br><img src=""paste-bc74d0b718f27ee6a72407e51ad913dff3609fe0.jpg""><img src=""paste-f6a3a57f3fdbdffea02520c21faf4f651b37bae0.jpg"">"										OB::trauma::AnkleFractures

{{c2::<b>Ulnocarpal Abutment Syndrome</b>}} is a common source of ulnar sided wrist pain secondary to excessive impact stress between the {{c1::<b>ulna</b>}} and the carpal bones (primarily {{c1::<b>lunate</b>}}). "Pathoanatomy of <u style=""font-weight: bold;"">Ulnocarpal Abutment Syndrome</u>&nbsp;is due to {{c1::positive ulnar variance (&gt;2 mm)}} which <b>increases wrist load</b> to the <b>ulna </b>({{c2::40}}% compared to {{c2::20}}%) causing early wrist arthritis."	"<img src=""paste-120bae5d367d77c9c488eba4dc495b538668757b.jpg"">"										OB::Hand::UlnocarpalAbutmentSyndrome
"<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">The&nbsp;</span>{{c2::triangular fibrocartilage complex (TFCC)}}<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">&nbsp;is a load-bearing structure between the&nbsp;</span>{{c1::lunate}}<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">,&nbsp;</span>{{c1::triquetrum}}<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">, and&nbsp;</span>{{c1::ulnar}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">head which functions to&nbsp;</span><b>act as a stabilizer for the ulnar aspect of the wrist</b>"	"<img src=""paste-74e3b49d5aee4c96da2e0a1d7de46d77434ad751.jpg"">"										OB::Hand::UlnocarpalAbutmentSyndrome

<b><u>Postoperative</u>&nbsp;</b>Type {{c1::<b>B2</b>}}&nbsp;vancouver is a<b>&nbsp;fracture around stem or just below it</b>&nbsp;with a {{c2::<b>loose</b>}} stem and {{c2::<b>good bone</b>}} stock; treatment is {{c3::<b>revision to long porous stem with fixation of fracture fragment (wire or plate)</b>}}	"<img src=""paste-79d82c38ecba72a45782084f8567d92acc7b16bc.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-f89d94ddd1cc34eb50585517bbd49a3af9feb37d.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::THAPeriprosFx

A {{c1::<b><u>Segond</u></b>}} fracture is a <b>bony avulsion</b> of the {{c2::<b>proximal lateral tibia</b>}} by the {{c2::<b>anterolateral ligament (ALL)</b>}} which is pathognomonic for an {{c1::<b><u>ACL</u></b>}}&nbsp;<b><u>tear</u></b>	"<img src=""paste-92558926237cfb7d6a7ca3948adb3a16a7fa0bf3.jpg"">&nbsp;<img src=""paste-7989ea637805a7ae6c092363af1fafec4417ba24.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-5700ab14c0a1bab4e9b7ed53696fd05dfd7cfde6.jpg"">"										OB::Recon::THAPeriprosFx
{{c1::Posterolateral}} approach to the ankle can be used for exposure to <b>posterior malleolus</b>, posterior ankle joint, lateral or posterior <b>fibula</b>, <b>peroneal tendons</b>	"<img src=""paste-7caf890c03a912e6e0ce3dad13045f8e31729a60.jpg"">"										OB::Approaches::Ankle::Posterolateral
{{c1::Posterolateral}}&nbsp;<b>approach </b>to the <b>ankle </b>uses the <b>internervous </b>plane between the {{c2::FHL}} and {{c2::peroneal muscles}}	"FHL is tibial nerve and Peroneal muscles (SPN)<br><img src=""paste-2e0f8a3e8c2ca00e59caf54a0b82c3733d78e334.jpg"">"										OB::Approaches::Ankle::Posterolateral
"Incision and superficial dissection in <u style=""font-weight: bold;"">posterolateral</u>&nbsp;approach to the ankle is made along <b>posterior border</b> of {{c2::fibula}}, care must be taken with proximal dissection to avoid damage of the {{c1::superficial peroneal nerve (SPN)}}"	"<img src=""paste-093ca6d861b23e6efb390968eae75667a5eba970.jpg"">"										OB::Approaches::Ankle::Posterolateral

<b>Ankle Arthritis </b>is degenerative joint disease of the <b><u>tibiotalar</u>&nbsp;</b>joint that can be broken into three main types: <b>osteoarthritis</b>, <b>post-traumatic arthritis</b>, and <b>inflammatory arthritis</b>; most common is {{c1::post-traumatic arthritis (&gt;2/3)}}	"OA is &lt;10%<br><img src=""paste-8452a852d4b07dbb41ed5df304f4ff31545f5499.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Arthritis
<b><u>Ankle arthritis</u></b> is classifed using the {{c1::Takakura}} classification<br>Stage 1: Early {{c2::sclerosis}} and {{c2::osteophytes}}; no {{c2::JSN}}<br>Stage 2: Narrowing of {{c3::medial joint space}} but {{c3::no::with or no}} subchondral bone contact<br>Stage 3A:&nbsp;<b>Obliteration </b>of joint<b> </b>space at the medial malleolus, {{c3::with::with or no}} subchondral bone contact<br>Stage 3B:&nbsp;Obliteration of joint space {{c4::over roof of talar dome}}, <b>with</b> subchondral bone contact<br>Stage 4:&nbsp;Obliteration of joint space with complete <b><u>tibiotalar </u></b>contact	"Stage 2<br><img src=""paste-c40777d5ccc9b4c997702530fbef46bcd1de3a4e.jpg""><br>Stage 3A<br><img src=""paste-3bdb8c5dafee16bc0204c6427b0b6b76c21ea62f.jpg""><br>Stage 3B<br><img src=""paste-044cb41abce516accd022d46db020e44f89d5434.jpg""><br>Stage 4<br><img src=""paste-9e6191826607b61f969462cdcd47d9f244617c9c.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Arthritis
First line non-op management of <b><u>ankle</u> <u>arthritis</u></b><u> </u>is <b>pain control (NSAIDs, activity modification<u>)</u></b> and <b>bracing/orthotics</b> such as {{c1::Arizona gauntlet ankle brace AFO::brace}} or {{c1::single rocker sole shoe::orthotic}} to improve gait and pain	"<br><img src=""paste-3031c0cdc1f974e56a7859a4b984a1e95d1aad07.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-d60f096a62aabdc7bec86ebb2925348c7318b4bd.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Arthritis
Surgical management for <b><u>ankle arthritis </u></b>is most commonly {{c1::arthrodesis}} in young, high demand laborers; {{c1::Arthroplasty}} is an option for older patients, but still undergoing further studies	Purpose of fusion is PAIN RELIEF; complication is subtalar arthritis (Adjacent joint disease)										OB::FA::Arthritis
Most common complication of tibiotalar (ankle) arthrodesis is {{c1::subtalar arthritis}}	"adjacent joint disease<br><img src=""paste-b566089d33306a3ceac81daaea03ac20b8a97722.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Arthritis
Primarily {{c2::medial}} space <b>ankle arthritis </b>such as stage {{c2::2}} or {{c2::3A}} <b>Takakura classification</b>, with <i><b>near-normal ROM</b></i> can be treated surgically with a {{c1::supramalleolar osteotomy}}	"<img src=""paste-ba664bba885cd0a407675e087914b67633ed4eac.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Arthritis
<b>Optimal position </b>of the <b>ankle </b>for <b>arthrodesis </b>is {{c2::neutral dorsiflexion}}, {{c1::5-10}}<sup>o</sup> of {{c2::external rotation}}, {{c1::5}}<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;of {{c2::hindfoot valgus}}, {{c1::5}} mm of {{c2::posterior talar translation}}	"Based off biomechanical analysis of optimal gait post fusion:<br><span style=""color: rgb(33, 33, 33);"">The patterns of gait showed that a valgus position of the arthrodesis is more advantageous and provides more normal gait, particularly on uneven ground. To attain more normal function of the knee and improve performance on rough ground, the optimum position of arthrodesis of the ankle appears to be neutral flexion, slight (zero to 5 degrees) valgus angulation, and approximately 5 to 10 degrees of external rotation. <b>Posterior displacement of the talus</b> under the tibia tends to produce a <b>more normal pattern of gait and decreases the stress at the knee.<br></b></span><br><a href=""https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3654697/#:~:text=Clinical%20relevance%3A%20This%20study%20has,10%20degrees%20of%20external%20rotation."">https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/3654697/#:~:text=Clinical relevance: This study has,10 degrees of external rotation.</a>"										OB::FA::Arthrodesis
<b>Ankle fusion</b> can be performed by {{c1::open arthrodesis (ORIF)}} or {{c2::tibiotalocalcaneal (TTC) fusion with retrograde IM nail}}	"<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">TTC fusion is&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">Load-sharing device with improved bending stiffness and rotational stability compared to plate-and-screw constructs<br></span><img src=""paste-7dc187eebd0863f24c64c041985fb77bc8f848e5.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);""><br></span>"										OB::FA::Arthrodesis
Mainstay surgical technique for ankle fusion is <b>ORIF </b>due to improved fracture reduction, high union rates but requires <b>NWB period </b>while TTC nailing is more minimally invasive and allows for <b>immediate weight-bearing</b> which may be a better option for {{c1::elderly}} patients with {{c1::neuropathic or charcot}} ankles or as a salvage for patients with {{c2::tibiotalar}} and {{c2::subtalar}} severe arthritis.&nbsp;	"Patients with significant hindfoot bone loss like post failed ankle arthroplasty or previous failed fusion also may need TTC nail.&nbsp;<br><img src=""paste-d64d84f1cfe82f323713527ead6b1bc6377f87df.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Arthrodesis
"{{c1::Hallux rigidus}}<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">&nbsp;is a common foot condition characterized by<b> pain and loss of motion</b> of the</span> {{c2::1st MTP}}<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">&nbsp;<b>joint </b>in adults due to <b>degenerative arthritis</b></span>"	"<img src=""paste-c7012c5944b2a23c22b98f661fd64a5aa0db9479.jpg"">"										OB::FA::HalluxRigidus

<b>Hallux Rigidus aka <u>1st MTP DJD</u></b> is most common location of foot OA due to biomechanics of <b>first MTPJ </b>carrying ~{{c2::120}}% of person's body weight with each step and <b>osteology </b>of first metatarsal as {{c1::shortest}} and {{c1::widest}}	"<img src=""paste-a31665f56e6d0465dc01f00b8906e558bec8caf7.jpg"">"										OB::FA::HalluxRigidus

The {{c3::Coughlin and Shurnas}} Classification can be used for <b><u>Hallux Rigidus</u></b> and is based on&nbsp;clinical findings of&nbsp;&nbsp;{{c2::MTP stiffness}}, {{c2::pain with extreme or mid ROM}}, and radiographic findings of {{c1::dorsal osteophytes}} and degree of {{c1::joint space narrowing}}	"<img src=""paste-b9d2414df3e1b4138fc069ae73e2274012dd6393.jpg"">"										OB::FA::HalluxRigidus

Patients with <b>hallux rigidus </b>who have<b> failed&nbsp;</b>{{c2::dorsal cheilectomy}}&nbsp;or are runners with<b>&nbsp;</b>{{c2::reduced dorsiflexion}}<b>&nbsp;(</b>{{c2::60<sup>o</sup>&nbsp;}}&nbsp;<b>required to run),</b> may be treated surgically with {{c1::Moberg Osteotomy (Dorsal closing wedge osteotomy)}} of <b>proximal phalanx</b>	"<img src=""paste-dc9fff1e687799345f10f002e9ad1b07835904a2.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-502cffacfd94822f270e15d38e01db89f1bbfec2.jpg"">"										OB::FA::HalluxRigidus

{{c1::Claw}}&nbsp;<b>toe</b> is a lesser toe deformity characterized by MTP {{c2::hyperextension}} and resulting PIP and DIP {{c2::flexion}}; typically involves <b>multiple toes </b>and is <b>bilateral</b>	"<img src=""paste-e107c33d5042ad9101600a0c9891f3f2cce44141.jpg"">"										OB::FA::ClawToe

Between non-operative vs operative treatment for achilles tendon rupture, new studies have shown {{c1::equivalent}} plantar flexion strength and {{c1::equal}} rates of re-rupture&nbsp;	"<img src=""paste-3c204f80b287ad5b4fa83acc50335250203c0698.jpg"">"										OB::FA::AchillesTendonRupture

<b>Zone 3 fractures of 5th MT base</b> occur in the <b>diaphysis distal to&nbsp;</b>{{c1::4th-5th MT articulation}}<b>,</b> associated with increased risk for <b>non-union</b> and is associated with {{c2::cavovarus}} foot alignment or sensory neuropathies	"Cavovarus deformity is high arch with hindfoot varus&nbsp;<br><img src=""paste-99c421f8a5489ac4add51f17af1344e5de115496.jpg""><br>Peroneus tertius inserts on dorsal diaphysis of 5th MT<br><img src=""paste-cd53ed695c919a1fff70a554ec58be05959dba73.jpg""><br><br>"										OB::FA::5thMTFx

<b>Zone 1 fractures of 5th MT base <u>without </u>rotational displacement </b>is managed with {{c1::protected weight bearing in boot/cast or stiff soled shoe}}	"High union rate by 8 weeks! The <b>Lisfranc ligament </b>tightens with {{c1::pronation}} and {{c1::abduction}} of the forefoot and is critical to stabilizing the {{c2::1st}} and {{c2::2nd TMT}} joints and maintenance of midfoot arch	"goes from medial cuneiform to base of second MT plantar surface<br><img src=""paste-0f8df41f132621bfa07c4857a710c62f9feb0c0e.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Lisfranc

The fracture table has been shown to induce an {{c1::internal malrotation}} <b>deformity </b>when it is used for treating femoral shaft fractures.	Compared to manual traction, the fracture table provides an advantage as only one assistant is necessary. However, it may limit access to the patient if other body areas are injured. Additionally, the fracture table has been shown to induce an internal malrotation deformity, which results from internally rotating the limb to facilitate access to the starting point and to improve visualization of the hip in the lateral plane. Specific pathological entities in <b>Dupuytren's disease </b>causes the following contractures: <br>The {{c1::pretendinous/central}} cord inserts into {{c3::flexor sheath at PIPJ level}} and causes {{c2::MCP}} contracture. <br>The {{c1::natatory ligament}} causes {{c2::web space}} contracture<br>The {{c1::spiral}} cord inserts distally into the {{c3::lateral digital sheet}} then into {{c3::Grayson's ligament}} causes {{c2::MCP}} and {{c2::PIP}} contracture. <br>The {{c1::lateral}} cord causes {{c2::PIP}} or {{c2::DIP}} flexion contracture. <br>The {{c1::retrovascular}} cord runs {{c3::dorsal}} to the NV structures and inserts on the {{c3::distal phalanx}} causes {{c2::DIP}} hyperextension contracture.
