{{c1::Scaphoid excision and four corner fusion}} is a partial wrist sparing procedure for treatment of <b>SLAC II or III </b>allowing wrist motion through preserved articulation between lunate and distal radius	"<img src=""6-Scapholunate-4-Corner-Fusion.jpg""><img src=""images-98e1796f8c99549a0f624ef60718ae230c4a180f.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SLAC"
<b>Wrist fusion</b> is indicated in {{c1::<b>Stage III SLAC</b>}} or any form of {{c1::<b>pancarpal arthritis</b>}}&nbsp;and has been shown to give the best pain relief and good grip strength at the cost of wrist motion	"<img src=""paste-83908e2d42280f71940230bf59864ae30ca57a8d.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SLAC"
{{c1::Scaphoid Nonunion Advanced Collapse (SNAC)}} describes the specific pattern of&nbsp;<b>progressive arthritis of the wrist</b> that results from a <b><u>chronic scaphoid nonunion</u></b>	"<img src=""snac_moved.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-39235b6b4e885c3631b7550307c93ef508a2f155.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SNAC"
<b>Major blood supply to scaphoid </b>is {{c1::dorsal carpal branch}} of radial artery which supplies <b>proximal 80% via retrograde blood flow</b> while the <b>distal 20%</b> is supplied by the {{c1::superficial palmar arch}} branch of <b><i>volar radial artery</i></b>	"<span style=""color: rgb(31, 31, 31);"">Blood flow to the scaphoid bone occurs via retrograde ('backwards') flow, meaning&nbsp;</span><b>blood enters at the distal (top) portion of the bone and there is no direct blood supply to the proximal (bottom) portion of the bone</b><span style=""color: rgb(31, 31, 31);"">. This retrograde blood supply is what can cause a fracture nonunion to go on to AVN&nbsp;<br></span><img src=""Blood supply TTC_moved.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(31, 31, 31);""><br></span>"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SNAC"
SNAC wrist radiographic classification<br><br>Stage I: Arthrosis localized to radial side of {{c1::scaphoid}} and {{c1::radial styloid}}<br>Stage II: {{c1::Scaphocapitate}} arthrosis in addition to stage I<br>Stage III: Periscaphoid arthrosis	"<img src=""paste-c9c09c3a4a62954215668c95ec64c4cdbd796f15.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-92ff17293e819e26221f92b77c9f0df50b0bde3b.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SNAC"
Operative treatment in SNAC wrist Stage I is {{c1::radial styloidectomy}} with {{c1::scapholunate reduction and stabilization}} or {{c1::distal pole excision}}	"<img src=""3-Scapholunate-RASL.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SNAC"
SNAC {{c1::Stage II and III}} is treated with <b>proximal row carpectomy </b>or <b>four-corner fusion</b> or <b>capitolunate arthrodesis</b> or <b>wrist fusion</b>	"<img src=""paste-42f6cc14dac4544603b10330f5dab7fa2041ca53.jpg""><div><img src=""6-Scapholunate-4-Corner-Fusion.jpg""><br></div>"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SNAC"
<b>Scaphoid fractures</b> are the most common carpal bone fracture most typically injured from FOOSH with axial load on a {{c1::hyperdorsiflexed}}, {{c1::pronated}}, and {{c1::ulnar}} deviated wrist	"<img src=""scaphoidfracture3_orig.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
Scaphoid fracures occur most commonly in the <b><u>waist </u></b>(65%), proximal third (25%) and distal pole (10%) although historically {{c1::distal pole}} was most common in pediatrics due to <b>ossification sequence </b>however waist fractures have recently become more common	"<img src=""Scaphoid_image5.png"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
In patient with suspected clinical scaphoid fracture with negative XRs, can {{c1::immobilize in thumb spica}} and {{c1::repeat XR and evaluation in 2-3 weeks}}											"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
Most stable non-displaced scaphoid fractures can be treated with<b><u> immobilization in thumb spica cast</u></b> as patients with &lt; {{c1::1}} mm displacement have union rates of ~{{c1::90}}%											"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
Unstable scaphoid fractures are {{c2::proximal pole fx (retrograde blood supply)::location}} and displacement &gt; {{c2::1}} mm which can be treated with {{c1::perc. screw fixation}}	"<img src=""blood+supply+is+disrupted+with+a+scaphoid+waist+fracture.png""><br><img src=""paste-73c3948abf5c2e52908919a0f4127c37bf3337d6.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
Incidence of AVN in scaphoid fractures is directly correlated with proximity of fracture to proximal pole:&nbsp;<br>proximal 5th AVN rate of {{c1::100}}%, proximal 3rd AVN rate of {{c1::33}}%	"<img src=""arthroscopic-treatment-of-scaphoid-fractures-nonunions-3-320.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
Dorsal percutaneous screw fixation in treatment of scaphoid fx compared to open dorsal ORIF has increased risk of {{c1::screw penetration of subchondral bone}}	"<img src=""paste-92ba71835a8623b9ebc7b60b26457a4c9f0cf122.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ScaphoidFx"
{{c2::Hook of the hamate}} is the {{c1::radial}} border of guyon's canal, the roof is the {{c2::palmar/volar carpal ligament}}, {{c1::ulnar}} border is {{c2::pisiform}} and {{c2::PHL}}, the floor is the {{c2::hypothenar muscles}} and {{c2::deep flexor retinaculum}}	"<img src=""3-Figure1-1-a2ebf41e00a41b024d852487fb9ecffcb80e8ca2.png""><br><img src=""https://o.quizlet.com/3LerZFBVr.BeCHWFnhb92g.png""><img src=""image899.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
<b>Zone 1</b> of guyon's canal is proximal to the bifurcation, {{c1::Deep motor branch of ulnar nerve}} is in <b>zone 2 </b>and lies under the hook of the hamate, <b>zone 3 </b>is <b>all sensory branches</b>	"<img src=""c002_f026.jpg""><br><img src=""https://o.quizlet.com/3LerZFBVr.BeCHWFnhb92g.png"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
<b>Hook of hamate pull test </b>is performed by holding hand in {{c1::ulnar deviation}} as patient flexes {{c1::DIP}} joints of {{c1::ulnar 2 digits}} against resistance, <b><u>positive with pain as flexor tendons act as deforming force on fracture site</u></b>	70% sensitive										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
Best radiographic view for <b>hook of hamate fracture </b>is the {{c1::carpal tunnel view}}	"<img src=""carpal_tunnel_view_superimposed.jpg""><br><img src=""carpal_tunnel_view_diagram.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
Nonoperative treatment for non-displaced hook of hamate fractures is {{c1::immobilization in ulnar guttar cast/splint}} for 6 weeks	"High non-union rates, but generally pain free<br><img src=""paste-0daa166944441ef6d1ca94a3fe42495b93fed809.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
Hook of hamate excision is indicated for treatment of {{c1::symtomatic non-union fxs}}, patients with {{c1::ulnar neuritis}}, or {{c1::high-level athletes}}	Faster recovery and return to play, most return to full activity by 6 weeks or sooner										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
Approach to hook of the hamate is volar wrist incision in line with intersection {{c1::ulnar border of ring finger}} and {{c1::kaplan cardinal line}}	"<img src=""surgical_approach_excision..jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
<b><i><u>Theoretically</u></i></b>, hook of the hamate fractures can be treated with <b>ORIF </b>to {{c1::preserve grip strength}} compared to excision. Screw must be {{c1::countersunk}} to prevent irritation of deep motor branch of ulnar nerve	"<b><u>Not routinely done, excision more common<br></u></b>Large hook of hamate fx excisions report decreased grip strength in some patients&nbsp;<br><img src=""orif_hook_of_hamate..jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
A rare complication that may occur in <b>hook of hamate non-union</b> is {{c1::closed rupture of flexor tendons to small finger}} from attritional damage due to proximity of displaced fracture fragment	"Yamazaki et al. present a case series of 6 patients with hook of hamate fractures that went on to nonunion and subsequently suffered closed ruptures of the flexor tendons to the small finger.&nbsp; They compared morphology of the fractures and found even small, rounded fracture fragments<br><img src=""paste-39a7f782d18bf56eb74e0a32d872cb5a4b574b6f.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::HamateFx"
What is the most common injured digit and which zone in extensor tendon injuries?<br><br>{{c1::Long finger}} and {{c1::Zone VI&nbsp;}}	"<img src=""extensor tendons.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ExtensorTendonInjury"
<b>Zone 3 extensor tendon injuries</b> can be tested with {{c1::Elson}} test where patient {{c2::flexes PIP joint 90 degrees over a table and extends against resistance}}, if unable to extend and DIP is {{c2::hyperextended/rigid}} then test is <b>positive </b>and {{c2::central slip}} is ruptured	"lateral bands and terminal slip pull on distal phalanx<br><img src=""central-slip.jpg""><img src=""CentralSlipExtensorTendon-sm.jpg""><br><img src=""Elson-Test-1.jpg""><br><img src=""extensor tendons.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ExtensorTendonInjury"
Patients with<b> Zone V (over MCP) extensor tendon</b> injuries may have<b> partial extension</b> due to the {{c1::junctura tendinae}} which connects with adjacent intact extensor tendons	"<img src=""junctura.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ExtensorTendonInjury"
<b>Nonoperative intervention</b> for extensor tendon injuries with <b>splinting in extension</b> is indicated for lacerations &lt;{{c1::50}}% and patient able to {{c1::extend against resistance}}	"<img src=""paste-6ce248b9fe42841db92eefb53a8aff2debe76b09.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ExtensorTendonInjury"
Extensor tendon repair can be done in the OR or in the ED, the number of suture strands that cross repair site should be {{c1::4-6}} to provide adequate strength for early active motion which is important to prevent stiffness; a {{c2::yoke splint}} allows for more increased early range of motion after repair of Zone 6 injuries compared to static or dynamic extension splinting	"use 4-0 or 5-0 nonabsorbable sutures; or can use one a modified bunnell stitch<br><img src=""6216tn.jpg""><br><img src=""screen_shot_2019-02-19_at_6.38.51_pm.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::ExtensorTendonInjury"
<b>Zone 1</b> flexor tendon injury is {{c1::distal to FDS insertion}} and results in jersey finger (inability to flex at DIP)	"Jersey finger also occurs with bony avulsions of FDP<br>There are 5 zones for flexor tendon injury<br><img src=""flexor_zones.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
<b>Zone 2</b> flexor tendon injury is from {{c1::A1 pulley to FDS insertion}}	"Zone is unique in that FDP and FDS in same tendon sheath (both can be injured within the flexor retinaculum)<br><img src=""flexor_zones.jpg"">&nbsp;<img src=""paste-c0eeb56d503014ab52739527a19fb370bf989e00.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
<b>Zone 3 </b>flexor tendon injury is over the palm from {{c1::distal aspect of carpal ligament to A1 pulley}}	"Often associated with neurovascular injury which carries a worse prognosis.<br><img src=""flexor_zones.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
<b>Zone 4 </b>flexor tendon injury is {{c1::over the carpal tunnel}}<br><b>Zone 5 </b>is {{c1::forearm proximal to carpal tunnel}}	"<img src=""flexor_zones.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
The {{c1::tenodesis}}&nbsp;<b>effect </b>occurs when passive wrist {{c2::extension}} should result in passive digit {{c3::flexion}}, passive wrist {{c2::flexion}} should result in passive digit {{c3::extension}} at MCP, PIP and DIP. If digit remains in extension with passive wrist extension, indicates<b> flexor tendon discontinuity</b>	"<img src=""paste-c832e2869fa9fbaffd18139640803f4b8b7ab63b.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
<b>Non-operative treatment for flexor tendon injuries </b>are indicated for partial lacerations &lt; {{c1::60}}% of tendon width, although may be associated with gap formation or triggering											"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
A partial laceration (up to {{c1::75}}%) of a flexor tendon has been shown to effectively be repaired <b>without gap formation</b> with an {{c2::epitendinous (no core strands)}} only repair&nbsp;	"Complete laceration of a flexor tendon should be managed with a core and epitendinous repair.<br><img src=""1-s2.0-S0363502317306767-gr1.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
Ideal suture purchase for flexor tendon repair is {{c1::10}} mm from the cut edge											"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
{{c1::Quadriga}} effect&nbsp;characterized by an<b> active flexion lag</b> in fingers adjacent to a digit with a previously injured or repaired FDP as tendons share a common muscle belly therefore excursion of the combined tendons is<b> equal to the shortest tendon</b>	"overshortening of previously injured tendon causes the effect<br><img src=""quadrigia 2.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::FlexorTendonInjury"
{{c1::Scaphoid ring sign}} is the rounded cortex of the scaphoid tubercle seen on a <b>AP wrist radiograph </b>due to<b> flexion of the scaphoid in SLAC wrist</b>	"<img alt=""Aggregate 111+ scaphoid signet ring sign - awesomeenglish.edu.vn"" src=""https://prod-images-static.radiopaedia.org/images/55008396/prova_jumbo.jpeg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Hand::SLAC"
PLC injuries are only isolated ~28%, typically occur in combination with cruciate ligament injury, more commonly: {{c1::PCL &gt;&nbsp; ACL}}	"<img src=""paste-4632daf910513a4c21a0ebcd98fc64d84b95b2b4.jpg"">"										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
{{c1::Popliteus}} works <b>synergistically </b>with the {{c1::PCL}} to control {{c2::external tibial}} rotation, {{c2::varus}}, and {{c2::posterior tibial}} translation	"<img src=""paste-7be7003312dfc7fe649bc77b2030f4a07dfa6ee4.jpg"">"										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
{{c1::Popliteus}} and {{c1::Popliteofibular}} ligament function maximally in knee {{c2::flexion}} to resist {{c2::external}} rotation	RESIST EXTERNAL means it promotes internal tibial rotation<br>These two structures provide the most rotational stability in knee flexion										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
{{c2::Reverse pivot shift}} test is performed with knee at 90 flexion with {{c1::external rotation}} of the tibia and a {{c1::valgus}} force is applied while <b>slowly </b>{{c3::extending}} the knee as the IT band transitions from being a flexor to extensor of the knee&nbsp;	"PLC test<br>tibia reduces from a posterior subluxed position at ~20° of flexion to a reduced position in full extension (reduction force from IT band transitioning from a flexor to an extensor of the knee)<br><img src=""paste-fc5d76b82660639bbaaee31c69cc92b4792b93aa.jpg"">"										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
Patient with <b>PLC </b>injuries may present with avulsion fractures of the {{c1::fibula (arcuate fracture)}} and varus stress XRs in {{c2::20}} degrees of flexion can be obtained to evaluate for PLC injury	"<img src=""arcuate fracture.jpg""><br><img src=""plc stress radiograph.jpg"">"										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
<b>Varus stress XRs</b>&nbsp;with {{c1::2.7 to 4}} mm of side to side difference: {{c2::isolated LCL tear}}, &gt; {{c1::4}} mm of side to side difference: {{c2::PLC injury}}	"PLC: LCL, popliteus, popliteofibular ligament<br><img src=""plc stress radiograph.jpg""><img src=""plc stress radiograph.jpg"">"										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
In treatment of multiligamentous knee injury, {{c1::PLC reconstruction}} should be performed {{c2::prior to or at the same time}} as ACL/PCL to prevent early cruciate failure; a {{c1::valgus high tibial osteotomy}} is indicated in patients with <b>varus mechanical alignment </b>and failure to address bony alignment causes cruciate recon failure	"<img src=""paste-f9cb1430779ff9d70c22ba5766eb39ec44701c44.jpg"">"										OB::Sports::PosterolateralCorner
<b><u>PCL Injury Classification&nbsp;<br></u></b><br>Based on posterior subluxation of tibia relative to femoral condyles at 90 degrees flexion<br><br><b>Grade I</b>: {{c1::<b><u>Partial</u></b>}} tear; {{c1::1-5}} mm posterior tibial translation<br><b>Grade II</b>: {{c1::<b>Complete</b>}} tear<b>;&nbsp;</b>{{c1::6-10}} mm posterior tibial translation<br><b>Grade III</b>: {{c1::<b>Combined </b>PCL + capsuloligamentous injury}}; &gt;{{c1::10}} mm posterior tibial translation											"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Knee&Sports::Knee::Ligament_Injury::PCL OB::Sports::PCL"

<b>Smith-Peterson/Ponte Osteotomies</b> can correct ~ {{c2::10}} degrees per level of osteotomy with more correction in the {{c1::lumbar}} spine due to {{c1::greater}} disc height&nbsp;	"These are PCOs (posterior column osteotomies)<br><b>less correction in the thoracic spine (lesser disc height and mobility)</b><br><img src=""smith petersen.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
Pedicle subtraction osteotomy (PSO) can correct ~{{c1::30-35}} degrees in the<b> lumbar spine</b> and ~{{c1::25}} degrees in <b>thoracic spine</b>	"<img src=""https://upload.orthobullets.com/topic/2041/images/pedicle%20subtraction.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
If a patient has an anterior fusion at level of osteotomy, which type of osteotomy is contraindicated?<br><br>{{c1::Smith-Peterson/Ponte Osteotomy; correction is at the level of the disc::and why?}}	"<img src=""smith petersen.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
If an anterior fusion is present at level of osteotomy, which osteotomy should be used?<br><br>{{c1::Pedicle subtraction osteotomy; correction is at level of vetebral body and not disc::and why?}}	"<img src=""pedicle subtraction.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-61d5be70000c29e22031f894176a0a378f8ccbaf.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
<b>Vetebral column resection</b> can correct up to ~{{c1::45}} degrees and is indicated for severe sagital imbalance beyond a PSO, typically in cases of tumor, fracture or infection	"<img src=""paste-27290269bb59b5359ec045c5f918fcebd142f026.jpg""><br><img src=""vcr.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
{{c1::Combined anterior and posterior procedures}} can be used for <b>large &gt; 70 degrees </b>of correction in <b>rigid curves </b>through anterior release, typically done at L5/S1 when fusing to sacrum	"<img src=""paste-09347ae70f93d65a1848563b48880ea9518e2de9.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
Overall complication rates for surgery in ASD are ~13.5% and higher with osteotomies, revisions, anterior/posterior procedures, and {{c1::VTE}} is most likely to result in poor clinical outcomes	"Have to weigh pros and cons of VTE PPX with risk of post op hematoma too<br><img src=""paste-6f8c1988bd4f7a2167aed0608fcf9fd2942dd1bc.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
<b>Risk of non-union </b>of surgery for <b>adult spinal deformity</b> most often occurs at {{c1::L5/S1}} and {{c1::thoracolumbar junction}} with risk factors including <b>smoking</b>, an anterior {{c3::thoracoabdominal}} approach, <b>osteoporosis</b>, kyphosis &gt; {{c2::20}} degrees, postive SVA &gt; {{c2::5}} cm&nbsp;	"<img src=""paste-a36ffe8139efdd2719ef3448c1286b39582c967b.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
<b>Instrumentation failure</b> in ASD is more likley to occur in bone with the lowest ratio of cortical to cancellous bone which is the {{c1::sacrum}}	"highest ratio in thoracic pedicles<br><img src=""paste-0a55b063127afaca1e67e86d18bad95bc6477c35.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
Radicular symptoms in ASD are generally on the side of the {{c1::concavity}}	"If symptoms progress to side of convexity, there is a worse prognosis<br><img src=""Scoliosis.-Concave-vs-Convex.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::ASD
In <b>Adolescent Idiopathic Scoliosis (AIS</b>) the most common curve is a {{c1::right thoracic curve}}, the presence of a {{c1::left thoracic curve}} is rare and should prompt {{c2::MRI}} to rule out {{c2::cyst or syringomelia}}	"<b>Syringomyelia is a neurological disorder in which a fluid-filled cyst (syrinx) forms within the spinal cord</b><span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">.<br></span><img src=""gr1_lrg-448748b6e227834e4cbdfbc3816341a7888d6ae4.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);""><br></span>"										OB::Spine::AIS
The incidence of AIS with curves<b> &gt; 30 degrees is 0.3% </b>and more common in {{c1::females}} with a {{c1::10:1}} ratio	"<img src=""paste-60e96500c8a478207dbc3d0256467a7fa133ccad.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
{{c1::Dejour}} classification is used for <b>Trochlea Dysplasia</b><br><br><b>Type A</b>: {{c2::shallow trochlea}}<br><b>Type B</b>: {{c2::flat trochlea}}<br><b>Type C</b>: {{c2::medial hypoplasia}}<br><b>Type D</b>: {{c2::cliff&nbsp;}}	"<img src=""https://www.researchgate.net/publication/322536539/figure/fig1/AS:606062025650176@1521507855957/Dejour-classification-of-trochlear-dysplasia-Note-the-4-morphological-variants-listed-as.png"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Knee&Sports::Knee::ExtensorMechanism::Patellar_Instability"
For MPFL reconstruction in <b>pediatric patients</b>,<b> femoral side</b> should be secured more {{c1::anterior}} and {{c1::distal}} to Schottle's point	"<img src=""schottle.jpg""><img src=""ajo046020076_f1.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Knee&Sports::Knee::ExtensorMechanism::Patellar_Instability"
The rate of recurrent instability after MPFL reconstruction <b>DOES NOT</b> differ with regard to graft choice (allograft vs autograft vs synthetic graft)<br><br>{{c1::True::True or False}}	"<img src=""paste-614e6e9e6c6244c1f257fed99c11a51b087824da.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Knee&Sports::Knee::ExtensorMechanism::Patellar_Instability"
Severe {{c1::trochlear dysplasia}} is the<b> most important predictor </b>of residual <b>patellofemoral instability </b>after isolated <b>MPFL reconstruction</b>	"<img src=""paste-c1a38a3e192203bc9a167c9c77b4ef076c4bd77e.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Knee&Sports::Knee::ExtensorMechanism::Patellar_Instability"
The goal of a <b>Fulkerson-type osteotomy (anterior and medial TT transfer</b>) is to correct <b>TT-TG</b> to {{c1::10-15}} mm, never less than {{c1::10}} mm, the anteromedialized displacement will increase <b>patellofemoral contact pressures </b>{{c2::proximally}} and {{c2::medially}}	"<img src=""paste-3aeb2f22b391e22ec4307cc46ce1c62ec336606f.jpg"">"										"#Ortho::Orthobullets::Knee&Sports::Knee::ExtensorMechanism::Patellar_Instability"
Pathophysiology of <b>Adolescent idiopathic scoliosis</b> is associated with abnormal develepment of the {{c2::neurocentral synchodrosis}} which is a&nbsp;cartilaginous plate that forms between the centrum and posterior neural arches and closes in the following order: {{c1::cervical (age 5-6)}}, {{c1::lumbar (age 11-12)}}, and {{c1::thoracic (age 14-17)}}	"<img src=""paste-e6431a186d1d0cab69e1d8001e23e0739dca09b1.jpg""><br><img src=""paste-b1cfa4527525d24ecb325a96234b8bbd61783bc5.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
The {{c1::Risser}} <b>staging system</b> is used to grade skeletal maturity based on the <b>level of ossification and fusion of the</b> {{c2::<b>iliac crest</b>}} <b>apophyses</b> and stage 0 covers the first {{c2::2/3rds}} of pubertal growth correlating with the <b>greatest velocity of skeletal growth</b>	"<img src=""paste-ea0a98ac6e3036edeab1baf05feb51960f7ee0b4.jpg""><img src=""screenshot-2022-11-16-144859.png"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b><u>Peak growth velocity</u></b> for AIS curves would be in females just before {{c1::menarche}} which is Risser stage {{c1::0}} which most closely correlate with Tanner stage {{c1::3}}	"<img src=""3-s2.0-B978012818872900011X-f00011-02-9780128188729.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
Which AIS curves are more likely to progress? Lumbar or Thoracic? Single or Double curves?<br><br>{{c1::Thoracic&gt;Lumbar, Double&gt;Single}}	"<img src=""47886-baaj_ais_lead.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
AIS curves &gt; {{c1::30}} degrees <b>prior to peak growth velocity (Risser 0, Tanner 3, before menarche) </b>have a strong likelihood for need for <b><u>surgery</u></b>	"<img src=""paste-1664809e9a917bd332ebd938841b4da4edaab7d9.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
{{c1::7}} degree curve on scoliometer during Adams forward bending test is considered abnormal screen	"<img src=""paste-1e3bd882e539a6862d5fffd868b8da98a8d20ae4.jpg""><img src=""ais forward bending.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Cobb angle </b>&gt; {{c1::10}} degrees is considered Scoliosis	"<img src=""applsci-13-03817-g001.png"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
In Scoliosis, the <b>stable vertebrae </b>is the&nbsp;{{c1::most proximal vertebrae that is most closely bisected by center sacral vertical line}} and the <b>neutral vertebrae</b>&nbsp;is&nbsp;rotationally neutral ({{c2::spinous process}} equal distance to {{c2::pedicles}} on PA xray)	"<img src=""end_image.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
Indications for an MRI in scoliosis includes {{c1::abnormal abdominal reflexes}} as a syrinx is associated with this	"<span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">The superficial abdominal reflex is elicited by&nbsp;</span><b>lightly stroking the 4 quadrants of the abdomen near the umbilicus with a wooden cotton applicator stick or similar tool</b><span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);"">. The normal response is contraction of the abdominal muscles causing the umbilicus to move toward the area being stroked.<br></span><img src=""paste-7d15c31006a3e66a79c374290a079d1f0b16c438.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(32, 33, 36);""><br></span>"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Treatment </b>of <b>AIS </b>is based on&nbsp;skeletal maturity of patient, magnitude of deformity, and curve progression<br><br><b>Observation Alone</b>: Cobb Angle: {{c1::&lt; 25}} degrees<br><b>Bracing (TLSO):&nbsp;</b>Cobb Angle: {{c1::25-45}} degrees for <b>flexible deformity </b>in <b>skeletally immature </b>patients, <b>Risser stage</b>: {{c1::0,1, or 2}}<br><b>Surgery: </b>Cobb Angle: {{c1::&gt;45}} degrees	"<img src=""paste-dcbb206680af04b8440f561d3def2db363aeee26.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Bracing </b>in patients with <b>AIS is dose-dependent</b>, with a {{c1::50}}% reduction in need for surgery with compliant brace use &gt; {{c1::13}} hours per day, NNT is {{c1::4}} in highly compliant patients&nbsp;	"<img src=""paste-dcecc4b4e9e6678dc6fe9c6bf97b1ba829e132b6.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Combined anterior and posterior fusion</b> is indicated in AIS with curves &gt; {{c1::75}} degrees and in younger aged patients (Risser grade 0, girls &lt;10, boys &lt;13) in order to prevent {{c1::crankshaft phenomenon}} which is <b>worsening deformity after posterior fusion due to anterior spine growth&nbsp;</b>	"<img src=""https://o.quizlet.com/tbmEQ.7kRdeYHxDKwuUFIw.jpg""><img src=""paste-c1ad063388abbc6f39b6e00ce32da2dc4cefc774.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
Brace Types:<br>Thoracic curves with apex above <b>T7</b>: {{c1::<b>Milwaukee</b> brace (<b>cervicothoracolumbosacral</b> orthosis)}}<br>Apex at T7 or below: {{c1::TLSO (Boston style (under arm))}}	"Braces must be worn as much as possible&nbsp;<b>16-23 hours/day</b>, minimum of&nbsp;<b>12&nbsp;</b>hours required to slow progression<br><img src=""7404.jpg""><img src=""PW-Boston-Brace-.2.png"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Skeletal maturity </b>is defined as:&nbsp;<br>1. Risser stage {{c1::4}}<br>2.&nbsp;&lt;1cm change in height over 2 visits 6 months apart<br>3. {{c1::2}} years postmenarchal	"<img src=""paste-d2574e796311207056908ead7ca5bf1849c071f4.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Bracing success in AIS </b>is defined as &lt; {{c1::5}} degrees of curve progression	"<img src=""paste-d2574e796311207056908ead7ca5bf1849c071f4.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Posterior spinal fusion</b> techniques:&nbsp;<br><br><b>Harrington</b>:&nbsp;recommends {{c1::one}} level above and {{c1::two}} levels below the end vertebrae<br><br><b>Moe</b>: {{c1::fusion to the neutral vertebrae}}<br><br><b>Lenke</b>: {{c1::include all major curves in fusion and minor curves that are kyphotic or not flexible}}	"Goal is that&nbsp;fusion should include enough levels to adequately maintain sagittal and coronal balance while being as minimal as safely possible to preserve motion<br><img src=""paste-95996cdbc5cdaa291f046db6bbb0db5ed0c36557.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
In <b>AIS </b>whenever possible avoid fusion to {{c1::L4}} and {{c1::L5}}, almost never required to fuse to <b>pelvis</b>	"Cochran found increased incidence of LBP with fusion to L5 and to a lesser extent L4<br><img src=""paste-1147887dabc9286f383c9eadf4a28d4bb1424705.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
In placement of <b>pedicle screws</b> for AIS, undertapping by {{c1::1}} mm will <b><u>increase </u></b>{{c2::insertional torque}} and thus&nbsp;<b><u>increase</u>&nbsp;</b>{{c2::resistance to screw pullout}}	"<img src=""paste-375e5cb8ff3340b99903e8af0f9cc49f903244b4.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
Neurologic monitoring with <b>somatosensory-evoked potentials (SSEPs) and/or motor-evoked potentials (MEPs) </b>is standard of care, a neurologic event is defined as a drop in amplitude by {{c1::50}}%	"<img src=""paste-a16e75d2c7c2565043dd6d9e1cd8989af5e99ea5.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
<b>Infection </b>after posterior spinal fusion has incidence of 1-2%, must commonly due to {{c1::c. acnes}}; treatment with I&amp;D with maintenance of hardware if it is not loose and within {{c1::6 months}}	"<img src=""paste-cb0ed7ab03502e933d790a5cd60b36d74d8c0f8b.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
Adjunctive short-term {{c1::steroids}} have been shown to <b>decrease opioid use</b> in post-op pain control after PSF for AIS.	"<img src=""paste-e46a7f5420f828d4d9270c7196973c06b286e893.jpg"">"										OB::Spine::AIS
{{c1::Bunionette}} Deformities, commonly called {{c1::tailor's}} bunion, are prominences on the <b>lateral aspect of the 5th metatarsal head </b>that most commonly occur as a result of {{c2::compression of the forefoot (tight shoes)}}	"<img src=""paste-5cb00b17e39f9a565994896b828b54f53b5e7370.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Bunionette
<b>Bunionette Deformity Classification&nbsp;</b><br><br>Type 1: {{c1::Enlarged 5th MT head or lateral exostosis}}<br>Type 2:&nbsp;{{c1::Congenital bow of 5th MT, normal 4-5 IMA}}<br>Type 3: {{c1::Increased 4th-5th IMA}} (most common)	"<img src=""paste-8d4837c18f6920fccf553e1302f7afb4350ad6c2.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Bunionette

<b>Type 2 and 3 Bunionettes</b>&nbsp;if IMA is &gt; {{c1::12}} degrees is treated with a {{c2::oblique diaphyseal rotational osteotomy}}	"Proximal ostetomy avoided due to poor blood supply in this region<br><img src=""14-Figure16-1.png""><img src=""paste-65313c077d672bc7bbd61b22b8ca7ac15fa7620e.jpg"">"										OB::FA::Bunionette

"In the pathoanatomy of hallux valgus the&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);""><b>metatarsal head </b>displaces&nbsp;</span>{{c1::medially}}<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">, leaving the <b>sesamoid complex</b>&nbsp;</span>{{c1::laterally}}&nbsp;<span style=""color: rgb(35, 35, 35);"">translated relative to the metatarsal head; this</span>&nbsp;displacement can lead to {{c2::transfer metatarsalgia}} due to shift in weight-bearing"	"<span style=""color: rgb(65, 0, 7);"">Transfer metatarsalgia is a condition that causes pain in the forefoot due to an imbalance of weight through the toe joints.&nbsp;</span><span style=""color: rgb(65, 0, 7);"">It occurs when too much weight is transferred from the big toe to the other toes, which can happen due to abnormalities in the first ray of the foot<br></span><img src=""paste-dcc7cb7fd9ccd90c957aa65f667b4411437f3d31.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(65, 0, 7);""><br></span>"										OB::FA::HalluxValgus

{{c1::Hindfoot malalignment}} is the most common reason for recurrent instability and failure after <b>lateral ankle ligamentous repair/reconstruction</b>	A patient with a native hindfoot varus will stress an isolated lateral ligamentous complex reconstruction and lead to an increased risk for surgical failure if the patient's hindfoot varus is not concomitantly addressed.&nbsp;<div><br></div><div>Hindfoot malalignment is the most common reason for recurrent instability and failure after lateral ankle ligamentous repair/reconstruction. Normal hindfoot alignment is approximately 5-7 degrees of hindfoot valgus with the mechanical axis passing through the medial aspect of the calcaneus, which would result in medial collapse if not for the strong, dynamic and static restraints within the foot that maintain our arch and the three points of contact during gait. When a patient has varus malalignment of their hindfoot, biomechanical forces are directed laterally and stress the lateral stabilizers including our lateral ankle ligaments and the peroneal tendons.&nbsp;</div>

Instrumentation in CRPP/ORIF of pediatric ankle fractures should be {{c1::transepiphyseal}} if possible as {{c1::all-epiphyseal (parallel to ankle joint)}} has been shown to {{c2::increase tibiotalar joint pressures}} and increase risk of {{c2::ankle arthritis&nbsp;}}	"<span style=""color: rgb(27, 27, 27);"">Fixation can be performed by an all-epiphyseal (parallel) or trans-physeal (oblique) screw orientation. Traditional treatment of this injury involved all–epiphyseal screw fixation (parallel to the ankle joint line) because it avoids the open physis, theoretically preventing growth arrest. However, a previous cadaveric study showed that parallel screw fixation can lead to increased joint pressures[</span><a href=""https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC8891663/#B7"">7</a><span style=""color: rgb(27, 27, 27);"">], potentially altering ankle joint biomechanics and increasing the risk of arthritis and complications in the long term[</span><a href=""https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC8891663/#B8"">8</a><span style=""color: rgb(27, 27, 27);"">]. Oblique screws may have an easier to achieve intra-operative trajectory, serve as a better fixation construct as they are relatively more perpendicular to the fracture line, and would not lead to increased pressure in the ankle joint articular cartilage.<br></span><img src=""paste-63a00ce8029a0c156dfd1489a88e63a80aaeab98.jpg""><span style=""color: rgb(27, 27, 27);""><br></span>"										"#OB::Peds::AnkleFx"
